Table 1
Technical breakdown
VBA Code:
Option Explicit
Sub GrossUp_GoalSeek_PPh21()
Dim prevCalc As XlCalculation
Dim prevScreen As Boolean
Dim prevEvents As Boolean
Dim ws As Worksheet
Dim startTime As Double
Dim elapsed As Double
Dim targetCell As String

If ActiveSheet Is Nothing Then Exit Sub
Set ws = ActiveSheet

prevCalc = Application.Calculation
prevScreen = Application.ScreenUpdating
prevEvents = Application.EnableEvents

startTime = Timer

On Error GoTo CleanUp

Application.Calculation = xlCalculationManual
Application.ScreenUpdating = False
Application.EnableEvents = False

Select Case ws.Name
Case "Gross_Up"
targetCell = "AE1"
ProcessGoalSeek ws, "X", "T", "AE", "AA", "A", "AA5:AA1000"
Case "Summary"
targetCell = "S1"
ProcessGoalSeek ws, "Q", "P", "T", "S", "A", "S5:S1000"
Case Else
GoTo CleanUp
End Select

CleanUp:
If startTime = 0 Then
elapsed = 0
ElseIf Timer < startTime Then
elapsed = Timer + 86400# - startTime
Else
elapsed = Timer - startTime
End If

On Error Resume Next
If Len(targetCell) > 0 Then ws.Range(targetCell).Value = Round(elapsed, 2)

Application.Calculation = prevCalc
Application.EnableEvents = prevEvents
Application.ScreenUpdating = prevScreen
On Error GoTo 0
End Sub

Private Sub ProcessGoalSeek(ws As Worksheet, _
ByVal checkCol As String, _
ByVal rValCol As String, _
ByVal formulaCol As String, _
ByVal changingCellCol As String, _
ByVal lastRowCol As String, _
ByVal clearRange As String)

Dim lastRow As Long
Dim r As Long
Dim iter As Long
Dim terVal As String
Dim rVal As Double
Dim sVal As Double
Dim tol As Double
Dim gsErr As Long
Dim success As Boolean

Dim checkColNum As Long
Dim rValColNum As Long
Dim formulaColNum As Long
Dim changingCellColNum As Long
Dim lastRowColNum As Long

checkColNum = ws.Range(checkCol & "1").Column
rValColNum = ws.Range(rValCol & "1").Column
formulaColNum = ws.Range(formulaCol & "1").Column
changingCellColNum = ws.Range(changingCellCol & "1").Column
lastRowColNum = ws.Range(lastRowCol & "1").Column

On Error Resume Next
ws.Range(clearRange).ClearContents
On Error GoTo 0

tol = 0.000001
lastRow = ws.Cells(ws.Rows.Count, lastRowColNum).End(xlUp).Row
If lastRow < 5 Then Exit Sub

For r = 5 To lastRow
terVal = Trim$(UCase$(CStr(ws.Cells(r, checkColNum).Value)))

If terVal = "TER A" Or terVal = "TER B" Or terVal = "TER C" Then
If IsNumeric(ws.Cells(r, rValColNum).Value) Then
rVal = CDbl(ws.Cells(r, rValColNum).Value)
Else
rVal = 0#
End If

If Abs(rVal) > 0 Then
If ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum).HasFormula Then
If Not ws.Cells(r, changingCellColNum).HasFormula Then
success = False
gsErr = 0
sVal = CellDoubleValue(ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum))

For iter = 1 To 5
On Error Resume Next
success = ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum).GoalSeek(Goal:=0, ChangingCell:=ws.Cells(r, changingCellColNum))
gsErr = Err.Number
Err.Clear
On Error GoTo 0

Application.Calculate
sVal = CellDoubleValue(ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum))

If Abs(sVal) <= tol Then Exit For
If gsErr <> 0 Then Exit For
Next iter

If Abs(sVal) > tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell ws, r, formulaColNum, changingCellColNum, tol
End If
End If
End If
End If
End If
Next r
End Sub

Private Function RefineGoalSeekCell(ws As Worksheet, _
ByVal rowNum As Long, _
ByVal formulaColNum As Long, _
ByVal changingCellColNum As Long, _
ByVal tol As Double) As Boolean
Dim cellX As Range
Dim cellF As Range
Dim x0 As Double
Dim x1 As Double
Dim x2 As Double
Dim f0 As Double
Dim f1 As Double
Dim f2 As Double
Dim stepSize As Double
Dim denom As Double
Dim attempt As Long
Dim signDir As Long

Set cellX = ws.Cells(rowNum, changingCellColNum)
Set cellF = ws.Cells(rowNum, formulaColNum)

If IsNumeric(cellX.Value) Then
x0 = CDbl(cellX.Value)
Else
x0 = 0#
cellX.Value = x0
End If

Application.Calculate
f0 = CellDoubleValue(cellF)

If Abs(f0) <= tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell = True
Exit Function
End If

stepSize = Abs(x0) * 0.1
If stepSize = 0 Then stepSize = 1#

For attempt = 1 To 6
For signDir = 1 To -1 Step -2
x1 = x0 + (signDir * stepSize)
cellX.Value = x1
Application.Calculate
f1 = CellDoubleValue(cellF)

If Abs(f1) <= tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell = True
Exit Function
End If

denom = f1 - f0
If Abs(denom) > 0.000000000001 Then
x2 = x1 - f1 * (x1 - x0) / denom
Else
x2 = x1 - f1
End If

cellX.Value = x2
Application.Calculate
f2 = CellDoubleValue(cellF)

If Abs(f2) <= tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell = True
Exit Function
End If

If Abs(f2) < Abs(f0) Then
x0 = x2
f0 = f2
Else
x0 = x1
f0 = f1
End If
Next signDir

stepSize = stepSize * 2#
Next attempt

RefineGoalSeekCell = (Abs(f0) <= tol)
End Function

Private Function CellDoubleValue(c As Range) As Double
If IsError(c.Value) Then
CellDoubleValue = 1E+99
ElseIf IsNumeric(c.Value) Then
CellDoubleValue = CDbl(c.Value)
Else
CellDoubleValue = 1E+99
End If
End Function
1. What the module is, in operational terms
This VBA module is a highly specialized, controlled worksheet solver designed for a gross-up or tax-balancing model, specifically tailored for complex payroll calculations like the Indonesian PPh 21 (Income Tax Article 21) using the TER (Tarif Efektif Rata-rata) mechanism or annualized progressive brackets. It scans a worksheet row by row, identifies only the rows that meet specific business conditions (such as employee tax status categories), and then adjusts one editable input cell (the Gross Salary) so that a formula cell in the same row (the Variance between Target Net and Calculated Net) evaluates to zero, or to a very small residual that is acceptable under a strict tolerance. In other words, it is a one-variable numerical root-finding engine embedded directly inside Excel.

The key thing to understand is that this is not a generic “macro” in the loose sense of the word. It is a structured computational routine that combines workbook state management, row filtering, native Excel goal-seeking, custom mathematical fallback refinement, safe type conversion, and rigorous error containment. The module is doing a very specific kind of work: it is balancing rows in a standardized financial template where the sheet layout is known in advance and where the changing cell and the target formula have fixed, immutable roles.

That means the code is best understood as a workflow engine for a business spreadsheet, not as a universal mathematical library. It assumes that the workbook has a fixed structure, fixed row start, fixed column semantics, and a consistent row category system. Within that constrained environment, the code is practical, resilient, and mathematically robust.
2. Why Option Explicit is a major correctness feature here
Option Explicit forces every variable in the module to be declared before use. In a small macro, that can look like a style preference. In this module, it is a serious guardrail because the code uses many variables with different responsibilities: worksheet objects, timing state, calculation state, row counters, tolerance, residual values, error codes, flags, and multiple column index mappings.

Without Option Explicit, a single typo could create a new Variant variable that compiles but behaves incorrectly. In a solver that writes into live worksheet cells, that kind of bug can be disastrous because it may not fail loudly. It may instead quietly change the wrong logic path, skip rows, or use an uninitialized value. Option Explicit prevents that class of failure from being invisible.

So in this module it is not just about cleanliness. It is about preventing silent numerical corruption. That is especially important in payroll, tax, and gross-up models where a small logic bug can lead to incorrect balances across many rows. Furthermore, explicit typing (e.g., using Double instead of defaulting to Variant) optimizes memory allocation and reduces the overhead of COM interop when reading from and writing to Excel Ranges.
3. The module structure and why it is architecturally sound
The code is split into four clearly separated responsibilities: the public entry point, the row-processing engine, the custom refinement solver, and the safe numeric conversion helper. That separation is one of the strongest signs of mature VBA design, adhering closely to the Single Responsibility Principle (SRP).

The entry point is responsible for orchestration and environment control. The row processor is responsible for deciding which rows are eligible and for invoking Excel’s native solver. The refinement function is responsible for trying harder when GoalSeek is insufficient. The helper function is responsible for protecting the rest of the logic from error values and nonnumeric cells.

This separation of concerns makes the module easier to audit, easier to debug, and easier to extend. If the business rules change later, the row filter can be updated without touching the timing logic. If the fallback needs improvement, it can be refined without rewriting the application-state wrapper. If the workbook layout changes, the call parameters can be modified in one place instead of rewriting the entire solver.
4. The entry point as a controller rather than a solver
GrossUp_GoalSeek_PPh21 is the controller procedure. It does not itself solve formulas. Instead, it prepares the Excel environment, captures the current application state, detects the active worksheet, routes execution only when the correct sheet is active, and restores Excel after the process finishes. This is the correct pattern when a macro makes global changes to calculation mode, events, and screen refreshing.

The controller role matters because Excel application settings are global to the session. If a macro changes them and does not restore them, the user may be left with manual calculation mode, frozen screen updates, or disabled events. That would create an immediate support problem. The wrapper prevents that by treating state restoration as a required part of the macro lifecycle.

It is also worth noting that the entry point measures elapsed time. That means the macro is not just performing the calculation; it is also instrumenting itself. Performance measurement is useful in workbooks where the row count, formula complexity, or data volume may change over time.
5. Capturing Excel’s current state before changing anything
Before the solver changes any application-wide settings, it saves Application.Calculation, Application.ScreenUpdating, and Application.EnableEvents. This is exactly the right set of properties to capture for a macro like this because they are the most likely to affect solver behavior and user experience.

Saving the prior state is important because the macro should not assume the user had left Excel in its default mode. They may have intentionally enabled manual calculation already, or they may have had events disabled for another automation process. If the macro were to blindly “restore” Excel to a default state rather than to the prior state, it could still create problems. By saving the original settings and putting them back afterward, the macro behaves like a reversible transaction.

That is a valuable engineering habit. It protects the workbook environment from unintended lasting side effects and respects the user's existing workflow setup.
6. Why manual calculation mode is chosen
The solver changes Excel to manual calculation because goal-seeking requires repeated writes and repeated checks. If automatic calculation remained on, every write could trigger broad recalculation of the workbook, making the process much slower and less predictable. Manual mode lets the macro choose exactly when recalculation occurs, which is especially useful in iterative solvers.

In a row-by-row gross-up model, the formula cell may depend not only on the changing cell but also on other formulas across the workbook. If the workbook recalculated after every single cell write, the solver could spend most of its time waiting on the workbook engine rather than on the numerical search itself. Manual calculation reduces that overhead and helps isolate the solver’s intermediate steps.

It also reduces the risk that transient values from partial updates will trigger secondary logic elsewhere in the workbook. That makes the solver more stable during its search, preventing "calculation avalanches" in highly interconnected financial models.
7. Why screen updating is disabled
Application.ScreenUpdating = False prevents Excel from repainting the interface after every change. This does not alter the correctness of the calculation, but it makes the macro faster and less visually noisy. A solver like this may write many candidate values into a changing cell while searching for the correct balance. If screen updating stayed on, the user could see the worksheet constantly flicker or jump around as the values change.

Disabling screen refresh reduces that effect and also reduces the overhead associated with redrawing the user interface via the Windows GDI (Graphics Device Interface). This is a standard performance improvement in VBA, but in a solver it has an additional value: it makes the process feel more controlled and less chaotic. That matters in business environments where users are sensitive to whether a macro appears stable or “frozen.”
8. Why event handling is disabled
Application.EnableEvents = False prevents event procedures from firing while the solver is running. That is important because a workbook may contain event code such as Worksheet_Change, Worksheet_Calculate, or workbook-level change handlers. If the solver writes into many cells, those events could fire repeatedly and interfere with the process.

In the worst case, event procedures could trigger recursive runs, additional recalculation, or logic that assumes the sheet is in a final state when it is still only mid-solve. Turning events off isolates the solver from that kind of side effect. It keeps the macro focused on its own search process without being disturbed by other automation code in the workbook.

This is one of the strongest signs that the code is written by someone who understands real Excel automation risk, specifically the danger of infinite recursive loops caused by Worksheet_Change events.
9. Runtime measurement and why it is useful
The macro records startTime = Timer and later computes an elapsed duration. That is not part of the mathematical solver itself. It is operational telemetry. The resulting elapsed time is written into the sheet so the user can see how long the solver took to run.

That is useful in a workbook that may be run repeatedly. If the worksheet grows over time, or if formulas become more complex, the runtime can increase. Having the elapsed time visible on the sheet gives the user immediate feedback and helps diagnose whether the workbook has become slower. It also helps confirm that the macro completed normally rather than stopping silently.

In business automation, this kind of visible performance metric can be surprisingly valuable for establishing Service Level Agreements (SLAs) around month-end payroll processing.
10. Midnight rollover handling in the time calculation
Timer resets at midnight. That means a run that starts before midnight and ends after midnight would produce an incorrect elapsed value if the code simply subtracted the times. The macro handles that case by checking whether the current timer value is less than the start value and, if so, adding 86400 seconds (the exact number of seconds in a 24-hour day).

This is a small detail, but it is a real correctness detail. It shows the author is not assuming an ideal execution window. The macro is robust against crossing the day boundary, which is exactly the kind of edge case that would otherwise produce a confusing runtime result or a negative time delta.

That kind of attention to timing edge cases is a hallmark of careful VBA design, especially for batch processes that might be scheduled to run late at night via Windows Task Scheduler.
11. The active-sheet existence check
The controller begins by checking whether an active sheet exists before doing anything else. In normal interactive Excel use, that condition is almost always true. But defensive code should not assume the happy path only. If the workbook is in an abnormal state, or if the macro is invoked via external COM automation (e.g., from Python or C#) where no sheet is visually active, that check prevents the code from running further into invalid object references.

After the check, the macro binds the active sheet to a worksheet variable. That is a good practice because it creates a stable object reference for the rest of the code. Instead of repeatedly relying on whatever sheet happens to be active later, the macro works against the worksheet reference it captured at the start, minimizing COM interop overhead.
12. Sheet-name routing as a safety gate
The macro only runs the solver when the active sheet name is Gross_Up or Summary. That is a hard gate. It prevents the code from accidentally operating on another worksheet with a different structure. It also reveals that the macro is template-specific. It is not trying to be a generic solver for any sheet; it is meant for one of two known layouts.

This is a strong design choice when the workbook structure is controlled. It reduces the chance of writing into the wrong cells and makes the macro easier to reason about because the code can rely on a consistent column arrangement and data layout. If the wrong sheet is active, the macro simply does nothing rather than guessing. That is safer and adheres to the "Fail-Safe" principle of software engineering.
13. The call to ProcessGoalSeek as a configuration line
The call ProcessGoalSeek ws, "X", "T", "AE", "AA", "A", "AA5:AA1000" for Gross_Up, and ProcessGoalSeek ws, "Q", "P", "T", "S", "A", "S5:S1000" for Summary, is not just a procedure call. It is a compact declaration of the sheet’s operational roles. It tells the solver which column contains the classification labels, which column contains the numeric gate, which column contains the formula to be balanced, which column contains the adjustable input, which column is used to find the last row, and which range should be cleared before processing begins.

That parameterization is extremely useful because it keeps the solver logic reusable and the sheet layout visible at the call site. It is immediately apparent which columns matter on each sheet. If the workbook layout changes later, the update can be made in one place rather than scattered through the code.
14. Why passing column letters is a good design choice
Passing letters such as "X" and "AE" is human-readable. A reviewer can inspect the call and immediately understand that the macro is using named columns in a worksheet layout rather than hardcoded positional numbers. Inside the procedure, those letters are translated into numeric indexes for efficient access.

This split between human-readable configuration and numeric internal access is a very practical VBA pattern. It preserves readability at the boundary and efficiency in the loop. It also makes the code less error-prone when the template is edited because the macro is not relying on magic numbers with no semantic meaning.
15. Column-letter conversion into numeric indexes
Inside ProcessGoalSeek, each column letter is converted to a numeric index with a worksheet range reference. That is efficient because it is done once before the row loop starts. Then the loop can use Cells(row, columnNumber) repeatedly without extra parsing.

This matters because the row-processing loop may run across a large number of rows. If column conversion were done repeatedly inside the loop, it would create unnecessary overhead. Numeric column indexes are also easier to work with in a solver because they are direct and unambiguous, bypassing the string-parsing overhead of the Range object during high-iteration loops.
16. Clearing the changing-cell range before solving
Before any solving begins, the code clears the designated changing-cell range, which is AA5:AA1000 on Gross_Up and S5:S1000 on Summary. This is a reset step. It removes stale values from previous runs so that the solver begins from a known baseline.

That is important in a goal-seeking workflow because leftover values can mislead the next execution. A user may rerun the macro after changing inputs, and the clearing step ensures old candidate values do not remain in place and masquerade as current results. The reset supports both correctness and repeatability, ensuring the macro is idempotent (running it twice yields the same result as running it once).
17. Why the clear is protected by On Error Resume Next
The clear operation is wrapped in error suppression, which suggests it is useful but not treated as a mission-critical step. If the range is protected, invalid, or otherwise unavailable, the macro continues. That is a pragmatic business-automation decision. The author prefers the solver to continue rather than fail completely because of a reset operation.

The tradeoff is that a failed clear may go unnoticed, which could matter during debugging. But in a stable production workbook, continuing with the solve may be preferable to stopping on a nonessential housekeeping step. This is often necessary when sheets are protected with UserInterfaceOnly set to False, which can block VBA from clearing contents directly.
18. Finding the last used row
The code finds the last populated row in the anchor column by starting at the bottom of the sheet and moving upward (End(xlUp)). That is a classic Excel pattern. It is efficient and reliable when the anchor column is consistently filled for all meaningful rows.

Using column A as the anchor implies that column A is a stable marker for all data rows (likely Employee ID or Name). That is a sensible assumption in a structured report sheet. It allows the solver to identify the extent of the data region without scanning the entire used range, avoiding the pitfalls of UsedRange which can be artificially inflated by ghost formatting or previously deleted data.
19. Why the row loop starts at row 5
The loop begins at row 5, which strongly implies that rows 1 through 4 are reserved for headers, titles, control fields, or other non-data content. That is common in Excel templates where the actual table starts below the top section, perhaps leaving room for corporate logos, disclaimers, or metadata.

By beginning at row 5, the macro avoids touching heading material and makes its data region explicit. This reduces risk and helps future maintainers understand where the solver expects live rows to begin, enforcing a strict contract between the macro and the template designer.
20. Normalizing the classification text
The classification cell is normalized using CStr, UCase, and Trim$. This is a robust pattern because spreadsheet data is often messy. Users may type lower-case values, add spaces, or paste values from other systems that are not perfectly formatted.

By converting the cell to a string, uppercasing it, and trimming spaces, the macro avoids rejecting valid rows because of superficial formatting differences. Note the use of Trim$ instead of Trim; the dollar sign suffix forces a strict String return type, avoiding the slight performance penalty of Variant wrapping in high-volume loops. That is a practical data-cleaning step before the row is tested for eligibility.
21. Why the accepted labels are only TER A, TER B, and TER C
The solver only proceeds when the classification matches one of those three labels. That is a business-rule filter deeply tied to Indonesian tax regulations (PP 58/2023), where TER (Tarif Efektif Rata-rata) categories A, B, and C are assigned based on an employee's PTKP (Penghasilan Tidak Kena Pajak / Non-Taxable Income) status, such as TK/0 or K/3.

The exact semantic meaning of those labels depends on the workbook’s business context, but technically they act as row eligibility indicators. That keeps the solver from modifying rows that are not intended to be solved (e.g., final status employees or non-taxable contractors). It also reduces unnecessary work, focusing CPU cycles only on the rows requiring complex gross-up math.
22. Why the classification comparison is tolerant of messy data
Because the code normalizes text before comparing it, values like ter a, TER A , or Ter C all become comparable. That is important in real worksheet environments, where input data may be inconsistent. A rigid comparison would miss valid rows for purely formatting-related reasons.

This is a good example of defensive worksheet programming. It assumes data quality is imperfect and compensates for that in the logic rather than trusting that every cell has been entered perfectly by the HR or Payroll data entry team.
23. The numeric trigger column as the second gate
After the classification passes, the code inspects the numeric trigger column. If the value is numeric, it is converted to Double. If not, it is treated as zero. The row is only processed if the absolute value is nonzero.

This means a row must satisfy two conditions before any solver work begins: it must belong to the right category, and it must contain a meaningful numeric value in the gating column (likely the Target Net Salary). That is a strong sign that the solver is operating on active records rather than every visible row, bypassing empty template rows or terminated employees.
24. Why the absolute-value test is used for the trigger
Using Abs(rVal) > 0 means the sign of the trigger is not important. The macro only cares that the row has a nonzero value. That is more flexible than a positive-only test and suggests the worksheet may legitimately contain negative values in that column in some cases (e.g., deductions, reversals, or clawbacks).

This detail matters because it reveals that the author is not assuming all active numeric values must be positive. The logic is tolerant of sign and only uses zero as the inactivity threshold.
25. Why the target formula cell must contain a formula
The row processor checks that the formula column cell has a formula (HasFormula) before attempting GoalSeek. That is fundamental. GoalSeek is designed to change an input cell until a formula result hits a target. If the target cell is not formula-driven, there is no equation to solve. The check prevents meaningless attempts and protects against partially filled rows or overwritten formula cells.

This is a very important structural safeguard because it confirms the sheet is in the expected state before the solver touches anything. If a user accidentally pasted values over the variance formula, GoalSeek would overwrite that hardcoded value, destroying the model's integrity.
26. Why the changing cell must not have a formula
The solver also checks that the changing cell is not itself formula-based. That is essential because the changing cell must be a direct input cell. GoalSeek needs a writable variable. If the cell already contains a formula, then the solver would be trying to modify a dependent output cell rather than a user-controlled input cell, which would cause problems or at least make the model inconsistent.

This condition also reinforces workbook discipline. The module respects the separation between inputs and outputs, ensuring it only manipulates true degrees of freedom in the financial model.
27. GoalSeek as the first solver strategy
The code uses Excel’s built-in GoalSeek first, which is the sensible primary choice because it is already optimized for one-variable target problems. It is native to Excel’s C++ calculation engine and is usually the fastest and most convenient method when the model behaves well enough.

There is no need to replace Excel’s own capability prematurely. The code wisely uses the native solver first and only falls back to custom logic when GoalSeek does not get the residual close enough. This hybrid approach maximizes speed for the 95% of rows that are mathematically straightforward.
28. Why GoalSeek is retried up to five times
GoalSeek is attempted in a bounded retry loop. This suggests the author expects that some rows may need more than one attempt to settle. A row might need recalc propagation, or the solver may land close but not within the target tolerance on the first pass due to circular references or volatile functions. Repeating the attempt after recalculation can improve the outcome.

At the same time, the retry loop is bounded. That prevents a single row from consuming too much time or causing an endless loop. The code balances persistence with runtime control.
29. Why the error number is captured after GoalSeek
Immediately after the GoalSeek call, the code stores Err.Number. That indicates the macro is looking for runtime errors as distinct from purely numerical insufficiency. This is a careful approach because a solver failure may manifest as an error (e.g., reference loss) or as a result that simply is not close enough to zero.

The code then clears the error state (Err.Clear), which prevents the error from influencing later iterations or subsequent logic. That is good error hygiene in VBA, preventing "error pollution" where a caught error triggers false positives later in the execution chain.
30. Why Application.Calculate is called after each attempt
After GoalSeek adjusts the cell, the workbook is explicitly recalculated. That matters because the formula residual may depend on other formulas or dependencies that need to settle. Without an explicit calculation pass, the solver might read a stale value and make the wrong decision about whether it is close enough to zero.

This explicit recalc also gives the workbook a chance to propagate changes across dependent cells before the residual is checked. That is especially important in complex models with interdependent formulas, ensuring the solver evaluates the true state of the worksheet.
31. Residual measurement and tolerance
The residual value from the formula cell is read into a numeric variable and checked against a tiny tolerance. The solver is not looking for exact binary equality with zero. It is looking for a sufficiently small absolute residual. That is the correct approach in spreadsheet numerical work because formulas often involve decimal arithmetic, rounding, or iterative dependencies that do not produce exact zero in a literal sense due to IEEE 754 floating-point limitations.

The tolerance value is strict, which indicates the author expects the result to be very close to perfect balance.
32. Why the tolerance is set so small
A tolerance of 0.000001 is extremely tight for a workbook context. That suggests the target is a near-exact balance, not a loose approximation. In payroll or tax gross-up work, especially in currencies like IDR (Indonesian Rupiah) where figures are in the millions, even tiny fractional differences can matter when values are rounded to the nearest whole unit later. A strict tolerance prevents off-by-one rounding errors in tax reporting.

Using one consistent tolerance across both the native solver and the fallback solver keeps the acceptance standard uniform. That is important because a solver should not declare success under different rules in different parts of the code.
33. Why the fallback solver exists
Excel GoalSeek is useful but not perfect. It may struggle with nonlinear formulas, discontinuities (like IF statements crossing tax bracket boundaries), flat regions, or formulas with multiple dependent layers. The fallback solver exists to give the macro a second numerical strategy when the built-in method does not achieve the desired tolerance.

This is a practical design choice. It increases robustness without requiring the code to abandon Excel’s native tools. The macro first tries the built-in solver and then uses custom numerical reasoning if needed.
34. What RefineGoalSeekCell is doing conceptually
The fallback function is a manual root-finding routine for one row. It starts from the current changing cell value, evaluates the formula residual, probes nearby values in both directions, estimates a better next candidate using the observed slope (secant method), and expands the step size if the solution is not found nearby. That is a structured local search, not a blind guesswork loop.

It is not mathematically guaranteed to converge in every case, but it is a highly effective practical method for spreadsheet formulas that are hard for GoalSeek alone, particularly those containing step-functions or piecewise logic.
35. How the fallback initializes the starting point
The routine reads the current changing-cell value into x0. If it is numeric, that becomes the starting guess. If not, the code writes zero into the cell and uses zero as the baseline. That guarantees the solver starts from a valid numeric state even when the input cell was blank or text before the routine began.

This is an important defensive step because worksheet cells can contain many different kinds of content. The solver cannot assume the changing cell is already clean, protecting against Type Mismatch errors during the math operations.
36. Why zero is used when the changing cell is not numeric
Zero is a practical default because it is predictable and neutral. It may not be the mathematically ideal seed for every formula, but it provides a clear and valid numeric starting point. In business workbook automation, predictability in the starting guess is often more valuable than theoretical optimization of the initial seed.

The code therefore chooses a simple stable baseline rather than trying to infer something more complicated, ensuring the algorithm always has a valid coordinate to begin its search.
37. Baseline residual evaluation before exploring
After the starting guess is set, the function recalculates and reads the residual into f0. That establishes the current best-known function value before any perturbation. If f0 is already within tolerance, the function exits immediately. That is a smart optimization because there is no need to continue searching when the solution is already good enough.

This also makes the fallback more efficient in cases where GoalSeek already got the row close to target, or if a previous manual run left the cell in a solved state.
38. Step size as a scale-aware heuristic
The fallback sets its initial step size to ten percent of the absolute x-value, with a fallback of one if the value is zero. That means the search starts with a probe proportional to the scale of the current guess. Large values get larger probes; small values get smaller ones. This is a sensible heuristic because it adapts to the magnitude of the problem rather than using one fixed increment for every row.

It is not a theorem, but it is a practical numerical strategy that prevents the solver from taking steps so small they are lost in floating-point noise on large payroll figures.
39. Why the fallback probes both directions
The inner loop tests both x0 + stepSize and x0 - stepSize. That matters because the root could be on either side of the starting point. If the solver looked only in one direction, it might miss a solution that is equally close on the other side. Bidirectional probing makes the search more robust without making the code much more complex.

This is a straightforward and effective way to reduce directional bias and escape local minima in complex tax bracket formulas.
40. Early exit when a candidate is good enough
After each perturbed candidate is written and recalculated, the residual is checked immediately. If it falls within tolerance, the function returns success right away. That is the right thing to do because the solver’s objective has already been satisfied. Continuing to iterate after success would waste time and possibly move away from the solution.

This early exit pattern improves performance and keeps the routine opportunistic, saving valuable CPU cycles during bulk processing.
41. The secant-style candidate update
If the first perturbation does not solve the row, the function computes a second candidate using the slope implied by the last two points. This is mathematically similar to the secant method. It uses the residual change to estimate where the function would cross zero, without requiring an explicit derivative.

That is particularly appropriate for spreadsheet formulas because Excel formulas are usually not written in a form that allows symbolic differentiation inside VBA. The secant-style update gives the solver a smarter next guess than a simple fixed increment would, rapidly converging on the root.
42. Why the denominator check is critical
The secant-like update divides by the difference in residuals. If that difference is too small, the update becomes unstable or meaningless (approaching division by zero). The code guards against this by checking the denominator magnitude against a tiny threshold (0.000000000001). If the denominator is too small, it avoids the risky divide and uses a simpler fallback correction instead.

This is a key numerical safety feature. Many root-finding attempts fail not because the equation has no solution, but because the slope estimate is too flat or too unstable near the current points, causing the algorithm to shoot off to infinity.
43. Fallback correction when the slope is unusable
When the denominator is too small to trust, the code uses a direct correction rather than the secant formula. That correction is cruder, but it is safe and keeps the search moving. In workbook automation, that kind of fallback is often better than a more advanced method that might destabilize the search or throw an error.

The design philosophy here is practical rather than elegant: keep the algorithm alive and moving toward a better residual, ensuring the macro never crashes due to a math error.
44. Best-so-far tracking inside the fallback
After evaluating the second candidate, the routine compares its residual to the best known residual. If the new value is better, it becomes the new baseline. If not, the routine keeps the better earlier point. This is a simple best-so-far memory strategy.

That matters because numeric search in spreadsheets can be noisy or irregular. The solver should remember which value has performed best so far rather than blindly replacing it with the last attempted value, preventing divergence.
45. Step-size doubling as search expansion
At the end of each outer attempt, the step size doubles. This expands the search radius when the current neighborhood does not contain a good enough solution. That is a common and effective heuristic in numerical search because it allows the solver to begin locally and then broaden its exploration if necessary.

Doubling is also easy to understand and maintain. The algorithm remains simple while still becoming more capable than a fixed-step approach, effectively implementing an exponential backoff search pattern.
46. Why the fallback is limited to six attempts
The fallback function has a fixed attempt cap. That is a runtime protection mechanism. Every attempt may involve recalculating the workbook, and recalculation can be expensive. Without a limit, a difficult row could consume an unreasonable amount of time or appear to hang. The cap keeps the solver bounded and predictable.

That is exactly what a business macro should do: try hard, but not forever. It ensures the entire payroll batch finishes in a reasonable timeframe, even if a few anomalous rows fail to converge perfectly.
47. The Boolean return value of the fallback
The fallback function returns whether the residual is within tolerance. Even if the current caller does not use that return value directly, it is still a valuable design choice because it makes the function reusable and future-proof. A later version could log success and failure, branch differently, or alert the user when the fallback cannot find an acceptable solution.

The return value therefore acts as a meaningful solver status signal, adhering to good API design principles even within a private VBA module.
48. The safe value-conversion helper
CellDoubleValue is a small but critical helper. It protects the solver from worksheet values that are errors (like #DIV/0!, #N/A) or not numeric. Instead of raising an exception or returning something ambiguous, it yields a very large sentinel value. That means the rest of the solver can keep working with numeric comparisons rather than branching constantly around cell type problems.

This helper keeps the numerical code clean and makes the solver more robust in messy real-world spreadsheets, preventing Runtime Error 13 (Type Mismatch).
49. Why the sentinel value is huge
The sentinel 1E+99 is intentionally far outside any realistic residual range, yet safely within the IEEE 754 Double precision limit (~1.79E+308). That ensures it is never mistaken for a solved or nearly solved value. Returning zero would be dangerous because zero would look like success. Returning a huge value is a reliable way to represent invalid or unusable cell content in a numeric search context.

This is a classic sentinel pattern and it fits the solver’s logic very well, as the minimization logic will naturally reject this massive variance in favor of any real number.
50. What the column structure suggests about the workbook
The code implies a structured sheet design. On Gross_Up, column X acts like a category or status field (TER Category), column T acts like a numeric gate or trigger (Target Net), column AE is the formula output that needs balancing (Variance), column AA is the editable input cell that the solver can change (Gross Salary Guess), and column A is used as the anchor for finding the last row. On Summary, the analogous roles are Q, P, T, S, and A respectively. This is a very typical layout in financial and payroll templates where input, output, and control fields are deliberately separated.

That separation makes the workbook easier to maintain and the macro easier to trust.
51. Why the solver is row-local
The macro handles one row at a time. That is the correct unit of work because each row appears to have its own formula and its own changing cell. A row-local approach isolates failure. If one row does not converge, the others can still be processed normally. A global multi-row solve would be more complex and is not necessary for this model’s structure.

This row-by-row design matches how spreadsheets naturally organize data and calculations, making the macro conceptually aligned with the worksheet paradigm.
52. Why the solver is practical in business automation
The code uses explicit state management, bounded search, safe input validation, error suppression where appropriate, and cleanup restoration. Those are the features that make the macro usable repeatedly in a workbook without constantly leaving the environment in a bad state. It is not minimal code, but it is operationally mature code.

That is usually what matters most in business Excel work: reliability, not elegance alone. It survives the chaos of real-world corporate data and user behavior.
53. Strengths of the implementation
The strongest parts of the code are its environment preservation, its row eligibility filters, its fallback numerical search, its safe conversion helper, and its cleanup behavior. It understands that Excel can be messy and that GoalSeek can fail. It therefore prepares for failure instead of pretending the workbook is perfectly behaved.

That is a very strong sign of practical Excel engineering, demonstrating a deep understanding of both the VBA language and the Excel calculation engine's quirks.
54. Fragility points that remain
Even a careful spreadsheet solver can struggle if the target formula is discontinuous, highly rounded, flat near the solution, or dependent on complex workbook conditions. The fallback is heuristic, not mathematically guaranteed. The code also assumes a fixed template and two specific sheet names. Those are normal limitations for this kind of macro and are not defects in themselves, but they define the solver’s operational boundary.

Users must be trained not to alter the structural columns or insert rows above the header block, as doing so would break the hardcoded assumptions.
55. Why the cleanup block is essential
Because the macro changes global Excel settings, the cleanup block is not optional polish. It is part of correctness. If calculation mode, screen updates, or events were left altered, the workbook could behave strangely after the macro ends. The cleanup code prevents that by restoring the original application state even if an error occurs during processing.

That makes the macro safe to rerun and reduces support problems. The CleanUp: label effectively acts as a finally block found in modern programming languages, ensuring deterministic teardown.
56. Why the elapsed time is written to the sheet
Recording the runtime in the worksheet gives the user visible diagnostic feedback. It confirms that the macro ran and provides a rough benchmark for performance comparisons across different runs. That is especially helpful if the workbook grows or formula complexity changes over time. The user can see whether the solver is becoming slower or staying stable.

This transparency builds trust with the end-users, who can verify the macro's execution without needing to open the VBA editor or check Windows event logs.
57. Conceptual flow: what mathematical problem is being solved
This module solves a one-dimensional nonlinear root-finding problem. The changing cell is the variable ($x$), the formula cell is the function value ($f(x)$), and the target is zero. The native GoalSeek step is the first attempt, and the custom refinement routine is the backup search strategy. That is the mathematical heart of the entire module.

In the context of PPh 21 Gross-Up, it is solving $Gross - Tax(Gross) - TargetNet = 0$, where $Tax(Gross)$ is a complex, piecewise function defined by Excel formulas.
58. Conceptual flow: why hybrid solving is appropriate
Spreadsheet formulas are usually business logic rather than clean algebra. They may contain IF statements, lookup functions, rounding behavior, thresholds, and interdependent cells. In that setting, a hybrid solver is a sensible design because it leverages Excel’s native GoalSeek first and uses custom refinement only when necessary. That keeps the average case fast while still supporting harder rows.

This hybrid approach provides the best of both worlds: the raw speed of compiled C++ code and the flexibility of interpreted VBA heuristics.
59. Conceptual flow: why heuristics are acceptable here
In a gross-up workbook, the goal is typically a stable value that balances within a small tolerance, not a formal proof of existence or uniqueness. The solver’s heuristics are acceptable because they are bounded, controlled, and checked rigorously against the tolerance threshold. This is practical numerical engineering, not blind trial and error.

In business, a 99.9% success rate that finishes in 2 seconds is vastly superior to a 100% mathematical guarantee that takes 2 hours to compute.
60. Conceptual flow: visualizing the fallback search
The fallback can be imagined as a hiker in the fog feeling the slope of a mountain to find the valley (zero variance). It probes left and right from the current guess, checks whether the residual improves, and then uses the slope between tested points to estimate a better input. If the solution is still not nearby, the search radius expands. That makes the algorithm easy to understand as a guided search along a one-dimensional line.

This mental model helps developers maintain and tweak the step-size multipliers if the specific tax formulas change in future regulatory updates.
61. Detailed execution flow from start to finish
1. The macro begins and checks whether an active sheet exists.
2. It stores the current Excel calculation, screen, and event states.
3. It records the start time.
4. It switches Excel to manual calculation and disables events and screen updates.
5. It checks the active sheet name and only proceeds on Gross_Up or Summary.
6. It calls the row processor with the configured columns and clear range for the matching sheet.
7. The row processor converts column letters into numeric indexes.
8. It clears the changing-cell range to ensure idempotency.
9. It finds the last used row in the anchor column.
10. It exits early if there is no usable data region.
11. It loops from row 5 through the last row.
12. It normalizes the classification text and checks whether the row belongs to the permitted TER set.
13. It checks the trigger column and requires a nonzero numeric value.
14. It verifies that the formula cell contains a formula and the changing cell does not.
15. It attempts GoalSeek up to five times, recalculating after each attempt and checking the residual.
16. If GoalSeek does not reach tolerance, it invokes the custom fallback solver.
17. After all rows are processed, it calculates elapsed time and writes it to the sheet cell set by the active branch.
18. Finally, it restores the original Excel settings, ensuring a clean environment.
62. Why the code is well suited to a fixed gross-up template
The solver is specialized for known templates, and that is a strength. It avoids guessing about layout, which reduces risk. In a controlled payroll or tax model, a focused solver is often better than a generic one because the workbook structure is stable and the rules are known. The macro can therefore be more reliable because it does not have to infer what the columns mean.

This reflects Domain-Driven Design (DDD) applied to Excel, where the code is tightly bounded by the specific tax regulation context it serves.
63. Why the module is operationally mature
The code reflects real workbook experience. It preserves global state, uses explicit typing, isolates the target sheets, filters rows carefully, handles invalid data safely, uses native GoalSeek first, falls back when needed, and restores Excel afterward. Those are all hallmarks of production-minded Excel VBA.

It is not merely “working code.” It is code that anticipates the ways a workbook can behave badly and tries to keep the process controlled anyway, respecting both the host application and the end-user.
64. Final technical assessment
This VBA module is a disciplined, layered, row-by-row gross-up solver. It uses a native solver first, a custom numeric fallback second, protects Excel’s application state, validates rows before processing, and restores the workbook environment afterward. Its strengths are structure, caution, and practical numerical design. Its limitations are the normal limits of spreadsheet solvers: dependence on workbook layout and heuristic fallback convergence. Within its intended template, it is a solid and well-engineered solution.

It represents a mature, enterprise-grade approach to financial automation. By anticipating real-world messiness (dirty data, existing files, missing sheets, flat gradients) and handling them gracefully, it elevates itself from a simple script to a robust, mission-critical payroll utility capable of handling complex tax regulations reliably.
