Option Explicit

Private Function ToDblFast(ByVal v As Variant) As Double
If IsError(v) Then
ToDblFast = 0#
ElseIf IsNumeric(v) Then
ToDblFast = CDbl(v)
ElseIf LenB(CStr(v)) = 0 Then
ToDblFast = 0#
Else
ToDblFast = Val(Replace(CStr(v), ",", ""))
End If
End Function

Sub Copy_Filtered_SCA_Data_To_IMPORT_v2()
Dim wsSrc As Worksheet
Dim wsDst As Worksheet
Dim srcTbl As ListObject

Dim compHeaders As Variant
Dim hdr As Variant

Dim visName As Range
Dim visGaji As Range
Dim visOther As Range
Dim visSev As Range
Dim visBpjstk As Range
Dim visBpjskes As Range
Dim visPPh As Range
Dim visTHR As Range
Dim visApproval As Range

Dim arrName As Variant
Dim arrGaji As Variant
Dim arrOther As Variant
Dim arrSev As Variant
Dim arrAmt As Variant
Dim arrApproval As Variant

Dim outAG() As Variant
Dim outAmount() As Variant
Dim outAH() As Variant
Dim outAI() As Variant

Dim totalVisibleRows As Long
Dim totalOutRows As Long
Dim dstIdx As Long
Dim areaIdx As Long
Dim r As Long
Dim rowCount As Long
Dim amt As Double

Dim prevScreen As Boolean
Dim prevEvents As Boolean
Dim prevCalc As XlCalculation

prevScreen = Application.ScreenUpdating
prevEvents = Application.EnableEvents
prevCalc = Application.Calculation

On Error GoTo CleanFail

Application.ScreenUpdating = False
Application.EnableEvents = False
Application.Calculation = xlCalculationManual

Set wsSrc = ThisWorkbook.Worksheets("SCA_Data")
Set wsDst = ThisWorkbook.Worksheets("IMPORT_v2")
Set srcTbl = wsSrc.ListObjects("SCA_Data")

compHeaders = Array( _
"Gaji", _
"BPJSTK Employer", _
"BPJSKes Employer", _
"Tunjangan PPh 21", _
"Tunjangan Hari Raya" _
)

If srcTbl.DataBodyRange Is Nothing Then GoTo CleanExit

On Error Resume Next
Set visName = srcTbl.ListColumns("Name").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
Set visGaji = srcTbl.ListColumns("Gaji").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
Set visOther = srcTbl.ListColumns("Tunjangan lain-lain").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
Set visSev = srcTbl.ListColumns("Severance Pay").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
Set visBpjstk = srcTbl.ListColumns("BPJSTK Employer").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
Set visBpjskes = srcTbl.ListColumns("BPJSKes Employer").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
Set visPPh = srcTbl.ListColumns("Tunjangan PPh 21").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
Set visTHR = srcTbl.ListColumns("Tunjangan Hari Raya").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)

Set visApproval = srcTbl.ListColumns("Approval code").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
If visApproval Is Nothing Then
Set visApproval = srcTbl.ListColumns("Approval Code").DataBodyRange.SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible)
End If
On Error GoTo CleanFail

If visName Is Nothing Then GoTo CleanExit
If visGaji Is Nothing Or visOther Is Nothing Or visSev Is Nothing Then GoTo CleanExit
If visBpjstk Is Nothing Or visBpjskes Is Nothing Or visPPh Is Nothing Or visTHR Is Nothing Then GoTo CleanExit
If visApproval Is Nothing Then GoTo CleanExit

Range("IMPORT_Data[[Name]:[Amount]]").ClearContents

totalVisibleRows = 0
For areaIdx = 1 To visName.Areas.Count
totalVisibleRows = totalVisibleRows + visName.Areas(areaIdx).Rows.Count
Next areaIdx

If totalVisibleRows = 0 Then GoTo CleanExit

totalOutRows = totalVisibleRows * (UBound(compHeaders) - LBound(compHeaders) + 1)

ReDim outAG(1 To totalOutRows, 1 To 1)
ReDim outAmount(1 To totalOutRows, 1 To 1)
ReDim outAH(1 To totalOutRows, 1 To 1)
ReDim outAI(1 To totalOutRows, 1 To 1)

dstIdx = 1

For Each hdr In compHeaders

Select Case CStr(hdr)

Case "Gaji"
For areaIdx = 1 To visName.Areas.Count

arrName = visName.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrGaji = visGaji.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrOther = visOther.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrSev = visSev.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrApproval = visApproval.Areas(areaIdx).Value2

If IsArray(arrName) Then
rowCount = UBound(arrName, 1)
For r = 1 To rowCount
amt = ToDblFast(arrGaji(r, 1)) + ToDblFast(arrOther(r, 1)) + ToDblFast(arrSev(r, 1))
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName(r, 1)
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "Gaji"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval(r, 1)
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
Next r
Else
amt = ToDblFast(arrGaji) + ToDblFast(arrOther) + ToDblFast(arrSev)
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "Gaji"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
End If

Next areaIdx

Case "BPJSTK Employer"
For areaIdx = 1 To visName.Areas.Count

arrName = visName.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrAmt = visBpjstk.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrApproval = visApproval.Areas(areaIdx).Value2

If IsArray(arrName) Then
rowCount = UBound(arrName, 1)
For r = 1 To rowCount
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt(r, 1))
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName(r, 1)
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "BPJSTK Employer"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval(r, 1)
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
Next r
Else
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt)
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "BPJSTK Employer"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
End If

Next areaIdx

Case "BPJSKes Employer"
For areaIdx = 1 To visName.Areas.Count

arrName = visName.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrAmt = visBpjskes.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrApproval = visApproval.Areas(areaIdx).Value2

If IsArray(arrName) Then
rowCount = UBound(arrName, 1)
For r = 1 To rowCount
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt(r, 1))
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName(r, 1)
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "BPJSKes Employer"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval(r, 1)
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
Next r
Else
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt)
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "BPJSKes Employer"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
End If

Next areaIdx

Case "Tunjangan PPh 21"
For areaIdx = 1 To visName.Areas.Count

arrName = visName.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrAmt = visPPh.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrApproval = visApproval.Areas(areaIdx).Value2

If IsArray(arrName) Then
rowCount = UBound(arrName, 1)
For r = 1 To rowCount
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt(r, 1))
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName(r, 1)
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "Tunjangan PPh 21"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval(r, 1)
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
Next r
Else
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt)
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "Tunjangan PPh 21"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
End If

Next areaIdx

Case "Tunjangan Hari Raya"
For areaIdx = 1 To visName.Areas.Count

arrName = visName.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrAmt = visTHR.Areas(areaIdx).Value2
arrApproval = visApproval.Areas(areaIdx).Value2

If IsArray(arrName) Then
rowCount = UBound(arrName, 1)
For r = 1 To rowCount
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt(r, 1))
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName(r, 1)
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "Tunjangan Hari Raya"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval(r, 1)
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
Next r
Else
amt = ToDblFast(arrAmt)
If amt <> 0# Then
outAG(dstIdx, 1) = arrName
outAmount(dstIdx, 1) = amt
outAH(dstIdx, 1) = "Tunjangan Hari Raya"
outAI(dstIdx, 1) = arrApproval
dstIdx = dstIdx + 1
End If
End If

Next areaIdx

End Select

Next hdr

If dstIdx > 1 Then
wsDst.Range("AG5").Resize(dstIdx - 1, 1).Value = outAG
wsDst.Range("AJ5").Resize(dstIdx - 1, 1).Value = outAmount
wsDst.Range("AH5").Resize(dstIdx - 1, 1).Value = outAH
wsDst.Range("AI5").Resize(dstIdx - 1, 1).Value = outAI
End If

CleanExit:
Application.CutCopyMode = False
Application.ScreenUpdating = prevScreen
Application.EnableEvents = prevEvents
Application.Calculation = prevCalc
Exit Sub

CleanFail:
Resume CleanExit

End Sub
1. What this macro is, in operational terms
This macro is a filtered export and reshaping routine for one structured Excel table named SCA_Data. It does not simply copy rows. It reads only the currently visible records, breaks each source row into one or more categorized output lines, and writes the results into IMPORT_v2 in a normalized import format.

The output is therefore a transformed dataset. The macro is acting like a small ETL pipeline inside VBA: extract visible data, transform it into category-level rows, and load it into a destination area for downstream use.
2. Why the routine is called Copy_Filtered_SCA_Data_To_IMPORT_v2
The name tells the story of the macro’s purpose. It copies data from SCA_Data, but only after filtering is already applied in the source table. It then writes the result to an import sheet. The v2 suffix implies this is a revised version of a prior macro, which often means the structure was updated after practical use.

The name is not just descriptive. It is operational documentation. It tells a maintainer that the macro depends on filtered source data and a specific import destination.
3. Why Option Explicit matters here
Option Explicit forces every variable to be declared. That is especially important in a macro with many range variables, array variables, loop counters, flags, and worksheet references. A single typo in a variable name could otherwise create a silent new Variant and corrupt the export logic without an obvious compile-time failure.

In a macro that writes financial or staffing data into a fixed destination, silent variable mistakes are dangerous because the output may still look plausible. Option Explicit reduces that risk significantly.
4. What ToDblFast does at a low level
ToDblFast is a defensive conversion helper. It accepts any variant value and tries to turn it into a numeric Double. If the value is an Excel error, it returns zero. If it is numeric, it converts it directly. If it is blank or empty text, it returns zero. Otherwise it strips commas from the string and uses Val to recover a number.

This is a practical helper for spreadsheet data because worksheet cells often contain mixed types. The macro can call one function instead of repeating conversion logic everywhere.
5. Why error values become zero instead of breaking the macro
In a summation or export context, a hard stop on a single bad cell is often worse than treating the value as zero. By mapping error values to zero, the macro can continue processing the rest of the row and the rest of the table. That is a tolerant, production-friendly design choice.

The tradeoff is that invalid source values are masked instead of surfaced immediately. But for an import pipeline, continuity is often more valuable than strict failure.
6. Why the helper removes commas before using Val
Users often store numbers in a human-formatted form such as 1,250,000. Val does not handle that format well if commas remain. Removing commas first makes the value more parseable. This allows the macro to work with pasted values and text numbers that visually look numeric but are still stored as text.

That little replacement step is one of the reasons the routine is resilient in real Excel workbooks.
7. Why the macro uses a worksheet object for source and destination
wsSrc and wsDst keep the source and destination explicit. This is cleaner and safer than repeatedly relying on ActiveSheet. The source is fixed to SCA_Data, and the destination is fixed to IMPORT_v2, so the macro always knows where it is reading from and where it is writing to.

That makes the code easier to audit and reduces the chance of cross-sheet mistakes.
8. Why the source is a ListObject
srcTbl is declared as a ListObject, which means the source table is an actual Excel table rather than a plain range. That matters because tables have named columns, structured references, and a DataBodyRange that reflects only the record area. This is exactly the right object model for a filtered export macro.

Using a table also makes the visible-row logic much cleaner, because SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible) can be applied to individual table columns.
9. Why compHeaders is the core routing structure
compHeaders defines the categories that will become separate output lines. The macro loops over that array and handles each category in turn. In other words, compHeaders is the category map that determines the export structure. The order of items in the array is also the order of processing.

That makes this array more than a simple constant. It is the control list for the entire row-expansion process.
10. Why the category list includes five items
The five categories represent the business items the export is supposed to emit: Gaji, BPJSTK Employer, BPJSKes Employer, Tunjangan PPh 21, and Tunjangan Hari Raya. The macro is not trying to export every field in the source table. It is converting the source into a smaller, business-specific set of payable or reportable categories.
11. Why the macro exits early when DataBodyRange is nothing
If the table has no body rows, there is nothing to export. Exiting early avoids later errors from trying to access visible cells or arrays that do not exist. This is a simple but important guard against empty-table states.
12. Why the visible-cell retrieval is wrapped in On Error Resume Next
SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible) throws when no visible cells exist. Because the source may be filtered down to zero rows, the macro temporarily suppresses that error while attempting to assign the visible areas. That allows the code to test whether each visible range is present and then decide whether to continue.

This pattern is intentional. The error suppression is not masking all failures forever; it is only protecting a known edge case during visible-range discovery.
13. Why each source column is collected separately
The macro retrieves visible ranges for Name, Gaji, Tunjangan lain-lain, Severance Pay, BPJSTK Employer, BPJSKes Employer, Tunjangan PPh 21, Tunjangan Hari Raya, and approval code. This reflects the fact that each column is needed for one or more output categories. Keeping them as separate range references makes the later loop logic fast and simple.

Instead of repeatedly asking the table for columns inside the inner loop, the macro resolves the ranges once up front.
14. Why the approval column has two header spellings
The macro first looks for Approval code, then falls back to Approval Code. That means it is tolerant of capitalization differences in the source header. This is a small but valuable compatibility feature because users often vary column capitalization while leaving the semantic meaning unchanged.
15. Why the code checks for each required visible range
Even if the source table exists, the macro still needs every required column to be visible and available. A missing visible range means the filtered table is incomplete from the macro’s perspective. By checking each one explicitly, the code avoids partial exports based on incomplete data.
16. Why visName is the most important visible range
visName is the anchor for all other ranges. The macro uses the name column to iterate through visible rows and to write the employee identity into the output. If no names are visible, there is no row identity to export, so the macro exits.
17. Why Range("IMPORT_Data[[Name]:[Amount]]").ClearContents is a meaningful reset step
This line clears the import area before writing new output. That prevents stale data from a prior run from remaining below the new result. It establishes a clean destination state so the output can be read as a fresh export rather than a mixture of old and new lines.

The macro is therefore not append-only. It behaves like a rewrite of the staging area each time.
18. Why the clear line is also a fragility point
This line is unqualified, meaning it relies on the active sheet context for the Range reference. In a carefully controlled workbook that may be acceptable, but it is one of the few places where the code could be more explicit. A safer version would fully qualify the destination worksheet before clearing.

That said, the rest of the macro is much more disciplined about object qualification.
19. Why the macro counts visible rows before allocating arrays
The arrays used for output need to be sized in advance. The macro counts all visible rows across all visible areas so it can allocate enough room for the worst-case output, which is each visible source row expanding into five output rows. This avoids resizing arrays repeatedly during the loop.
20. Why totalOutRows is computed by multiplying visible rows by category count
Each visible source row may produce one output row per category. The formula uses the number of categories in compHeaders to create a safe upper bound. This ensures the output arrays are large enough for every possible generated line.
21. Why the output arrays are one-column arrays
outAG, outAmount, outAH, and outAI each represent one destination column. This design fits Excel’s bulk range assignment model. The macro builds each destination column in memory and then writes each array in one shot, which is much faster than cell-by-cell writing.
22. Why there are four output arrays rather than one multidimensional table array
Separate column arrays simplify logic because each destination column has a distinct meaning: name, amount, category, and approval code. A single 2D output table could also work, but the separate arrays make the code easier to read and keep the category-specific assignments simple.
23. Why dstIdx starts at 1
VBA arrays are 1-based here because they are explicitly redimensioned from 1 To totalOutRows. dstIdx therefore points to the next available row in the output arrays. Starting at 1 matches the array bounds and keeps the indexing straightforward.
24. Why dstIdx is shared across all categories
The output is a single stacked list. There is no separate destination block for each category. A single shared pointer lets the macro append each generated line into the same contiguous output sequence regardless of category.
25. Why the loop over compHeaders is outermost
The macro processes the same category for all visible rows before moving to the next category. That means the final output is grouped by category rather than by source row. This choice may be useful if the downstream import or review expects category batches.
26. Why the category handling uses Select Case
Select Case is the cleanest way to branch on a fixed list of string categories. It makes the intent visible and avoids a long chain of If ... ElseIf ... statements. Each category branch is easy to read because its data source and output label are explicit.
27. Why the Gaji branch is special
Gaji is not a direct one-column export. It is computed as Gaji + Tunjangan lain-lain + Severance Pay. That means the output category Gaji is actually a composite payroll amount. The macro therefore treats it differently from the other categories, which are single-field exports.
28. Why the Gaji calculation includes Tunjangan lain-lain and Severance Pay
The source structure appears to split total pay into several components that should be grouped under the exported Gaji line. By summing them, the macro converts detailed source components into one reportable amount that matches the import model. This is a business-rule decision, not just arithmetic.
29. Why arrName, arrGaji, arrOther, arrSev, and arrApproval are loaded per area
Filtered ranges in Excel can be split into multiple non-contiguous visible areas. The macro loads values area by area because the visible rows may not exist as a single continuous block. This keeps row alignment correct within each visible segment.
30. Why the code checks IsArray(arrName)
If a visible area contains only one cell, Value2 can come back as a scalar instead of an array. The code must handle both forms. IsArray(arrName) distinguishes the multi-row case from the single-cell case so the macro can process either one safely.
31. Why the single-cell path exists in every category
Filtered data can collapse to one visible row. In that state, looping with UBound would fail unless the macro detects the scalar case first. The dedicated else branch ensures the macro still works even for a one-row filtered result.
32. Why rowCount = UBound(arrName, 1) is used
Once the visible data is known to be an array, the first dimension of the array corresponds to the number of rows. UBound(arrName, 1) gives the row count within that visible area. That is the correct way to loop through a table slice loaded by Value2.
33. Why the macro calculates amt before checking it
The amount is assembled from one or more source values and then tested against zero. This makes the skip rule simple: only nonzero amounts are exported. The macro therefore avoids filling the import sheet with empty or meaningless lines.
34. Why amt <> 0# is the filter condition
The macro uses zero as the inactivity threshold. If the computed amount is not zero, it is exported. This is a pragmatic choice because the output is intended to represent meaningful payable or reportable items, not every possible row-category combination.
35. Why the amount conversion is done with ToDblFast inside each branch
Every source field can contain text, blanks, or errors. Calling the helper inside the loop keeps the code safe while minimizing repeated conversion logic. It also makes each branch self-contained and easy to audit.
36. Why the BPJSTK Employer branch reads one column only
This category appears to map directly to a single source field. The macro therefore does not need to aggregate other components. It just converts the field, checks for zero, and writes the result with the category label.
37. Why the BPJSKes Employer branch is separate from BPJSTK Employer
Even though both are employer-related contributions, they are distinct business concepts. The macro keeps them separate because downstream processing or reporting likely expects them as different categories. Preserving that distinction avoids flattening important payroll meaning.
38. Why Tunjangan PPh 21 remains its own category
This line item likely relates to tax support or tax gross-up treatment. It is not mixed into salary or employer contribution totals because it has a different accounting or reporting meaning. The macro keeps it isolated so the import format can reflect that meaning directly.
39. Why Tunjangan Hari Raya remains separate
THR is a distinct compensation item. Exporting it as its own category preserves clarity and avoids hiding it inside a broader salary bucket. That is especially useful for payroll reporting or downstream budget mapping.
40. Why the output row structure is normalized
Each output line has one name, one amount, one category, and one approval code. This is a normalized line-item format, which is easier to import into other systems or pivot later. The macro is not preserving the source table’s wide structure. It is converting it into a structured transaction-style list.
41. Why the macro writes employee names repeatedly
Because one source row can generate several output rows, the employee name must be repeated on each line so the output remains traceable. That is normal in flattened line-item exports. Without repetition, the downstream data would lose row identity.
42. Why the approval code is repeated on each generated line
Approval context belongs to the source record and should travel with every generated category row derived from that record. Repeating the approval code preserves control metadata on each line, which is useful for review, filtering, or import validation later.
43. Why the output arrays are populated only after the nonzero test
This prevents empty or zero-value lines from consuming output rows. It keeps the final dataset smaller and more relevant. The logic also means the output count can be less than the maximum allocation, which is why the final write uses dstIdx - 1 rather than totalOutRows.
44. Why the macro only writes output if dstIdx > 1
If no qualifying records were exported, dstIdx remains at 1. The test prevents a zero-row write from happening. That is a simple and safe guard against writing an empty range.
45. Why the final write uses .Resize(dstIdx - 1, 1)
dstIdx - 1 is the actual number of populated rows because dstIdx always points to the next free slot. Writing only the populated portion avoids trailing blank rows in the destination. The macro therefore writes exactly what it produced, no more and no less.
46. Why the four writes happen separately
Each output array maps to a separate destination column. Writing them independently is simple and fast. Because they were built in parallel with the same row count, each column aligns correctly with the others when written to the sheet.
47. Why this is a high-performance VBA pattern
The macro uses worksheet ranges to gather visible cells, but then shifts the heavy work into arrays. That minimizes repeated reads and writes against the Excel object model. Bulk read, in-memory transform, bulk write is the correct performance pattern for VBA.
48. Why Application.ScreenUpdating = False helps here
Without screen updating disabled, the sheet could visibly redraw while the macro is building and writing the export. That slows things down and can make the macro feel unstable. Turning screen updates off keeps the process visually quiet and faster.
49. Why Application.EnableEvents = False helps here
If the workbook contains change events or other handlers, writing a large block to the destination could trigger them. That can interfere with the export or even cause recursion. Disabling events isolates this macro from other automation logic in the workbook.
50. Why manual calculation is used
Because the macro is not depending on formulas during the export process, it can safely suppress automatic recalculation while it works. This improves speed and reduces the chance that intermediate writes trigger unnecessary recalculation.
51. Why the previous application state is stored first
The macro captures the current screen, event, and calculation settings before changing them. That allows it to restore the workbook environment exactly as it was before the macro ran. This is a core correctness property for any macro that changes global Excel settings.
52. Why restoration belongs in CleanExit
The cleanup path runs both after successful processing and after failures. That ensures Excel is returned to a normal state even if the macro exits early or encounters an error. The restoration is therefore part of the macro’s correctness, not just a polish step.
53. Why CleanFail simply resumes cleanup
The macro does not attempt elaborate error recovery. It assumes that restoring Excel state is the most important thing after a failure. That is a pragmatic choice for an export routine whose main job is to avoid leaving Excel in a bad state.
54. Why On Error GoTo CleanFail is placed after saving state
The application state must be saved before any risky operations, so that even if an error happens later, the cleanup can restore the original settings. This order matters. Without it, the macro could lose the information needed to restore Excel correctly.
55. Why visApproval gets a fallback header spelling
The approval code is likely an important business control field, so the macro tries hard to find it even if capitalization differs. That reduces brittleness in the source schema. It is a good example of targeted tolerance rather than broad guesswork.
56. Why the macro checks all required fields before clearing the destination
It does not clear and then discover that a source column is missing. It validates the required visible ranges first and only proceeds when all required inputs are available. That helps avoid unnecessary deletion of prior destination data when the source is not ready.
57. Why totalVisibleRows is accumulated across Areas
Filtered ranges can be split into multiple visible chunks. Counting the rows across all areas produces the true number of visible records. That is necessary to dimension the output arrays correctly before writing.
58. Why the macro does not process hidden rows
The use of SpecialCells(xlCellTypeVisible) means the export respects the filter state. Hidden rows are excluded intentionally. This makes the export reflect exactly what the user has currently filtered into view.
59. Why the filter state is part of the business logic
The macro is not operating on all data in the source table. It is operating on the user-selected subset represented by visible rows. That means the user can control the export scope through filtering without changing the macro itself.
60. Why the source table must have the expected headers
The macro relies on fixed column names. If the source headers change materially, the ListColumns(...) lookups will fail. This is a template-driven macro, so the input schema is part of the contract.
61. Why this is acceptable in a controlled workbook
When the workbook is designed around a known table structure, explicit header names are preferable to dynamic inference. They make the code easier to read and less likely to misclassify data. The macro is therefore optimized for stability within a fixed template rather than for flexibility across arbitrary sheets.
62. Why the macro is a transformation engine, not a simple copy routine
It does not preserve rows as-is. It expands, collapses, labels, and filters them. That is transformation. The source table is just the starting point for a more specialized import dataset.
63. Why the Gaji branch demonstrates business-rule encoding
It shows that the output category names are not just mirrors of source columns. They can represent a composed business concept. The macro’s logic knows that Gaji in the export should include other components, which is a meaningful business rule rather than a mechanical copy.
64. Why the Gaji branch is the most complex one
It must read three source values and sum them before writing one export line. The other branches read one source value each. This makes Gaji the only branch that performs a multi-field aggregation step.
65. Why the macro can be thought of as a flattening routine
It takes a wider source table and converts it into a narrow line-item output. Each source row becomes several export rows, each carrying a category label. That is classic flattening, which is useful when downstream systems want one item per line rather than a wide matrix of fields.
66. Why the output likely matches an import schema
The destination name IMPORT_v2 and the range columns Name through Amount suggest the output is meant for another process or workbook step. The macro is shaping the data to fit a predefined import layout rather than for immediate human viewing only.
67. Why the code avoids repeated worksheet accesses inside inner loops
By loading visible columns into arrays before iterating, the macro avoids expensive cell-by-cell reads. This is one of the biggest performance improvements available in VBA. The inner loops operate on memory values, not on worksheet objects.
68. Why the macro is robust to a single visible row
Filtered tables often collapse to one row. The code explicitly handles scalar values instead of assuming arrays always exist. That means the export can run even when only one employee record matches the filter.
69. Why the macro handles multiple visible areas
Because filtered data can be discontiguous, the code processes each visible area separately. That prevents row misalignment and ensures every visible segment contributes to the export. This is a correct response to how Excel actually returns filtered ranges.
70. Why arrApproval is reloaded in every branch
Approval values need to line up with the current visible area and row count. Reloading them inside each area loop keeps the values aligned with the source rows being processed. It also keeps the branch logic self-contained.
71. Why the output labels are hardcoded strings
The labels are business-defined categories, and the macro wants them to be stable. Hardcoding them ensures consistency and prevents accidental spelling changes from appearing in the output. That matters because downstream systems may depend on exact label text.
72. Why BPJSTK Employer and BPJSKes Employer are not merged into one total
Even though both are employer-related, they likely have different accounting or reporting meanings. The macro preserves that distinction explicitly. That is a sign the code is built for business clarity, not just arithmetic compactness.
73. Why Tunjangan PPh 21 is preserved separately from Gaji
Tax-related allowance should remain visible as its own category rather than being folded into salary. That separation helps with auditability and with later import processing. It also matches the way payroll components are commonly tracked in practice.
74. Why Tunjangan Hari Raya gets its own branch
THR is a distinct payroll event in many environments. The macro treats it as a first-class category, which means the output can capture it directly without inferential logic. That is the right approach for payroll exports.
75. Why the macro is good for downstream reconciliation
Because each line carries category, amount, and identity, the output can be checked against source data or imported into another workbook for review. The format is sufficiently normalized for filtering, summing, or pivoting.
76. Why the macro’s structure makes it easy to audit
Each source column is named directly, and each destination label is written directly. There is very little implicit behavior. That means a reviewer can trace the entire transformation path from source fields to output rows.
77. Why the code is not over-abstracted
It could have used additional helper procedures to reduce repetition, but it intentionally keeps the category branches visible. That makes the business logic easier to read. Sometimes explicit repetition is better than hiding branching behavior inside too much abstraction.
78. Why the code is still maintainable despite repetition
Although the category branches look similar, they are easy to compare because they follow the same pattern. This consistency makes future edits easier. If a new category is added later, the developer can mirror the existing structure.
79. Why the source schema should be treated as contractual
The macro is built around fixed header names and a fixed table object. That means the workbook’s source table is part of the macro’s contract. Users changing those headers without updating the code will break the export.
80. Why the code is operationally mature
It saves and restores application state, handles empty tables, processes filtered visible rows, handles multiple areas, uses in-memory arrays, skips zeros, and writes back in bulk. Those are all signs of a macro written for real workbook operations rather than a toy example.
81. Why the main remaining risk is the unqualified destination clear
Most of the macro is explicit about object references, but the clear line depends on context. That is the one place where a workbook rename, active-sheet change, or table name confusion could create trouble. It is the clearest improvement point in the current design.
82. Why the macro is well suited to a staging sheet
A staging sheet is exactly where normalized import rows belong. The macro prepares a clean, repeatable output area and replaces prior contents each run. That is ideal for a temporary import buffer.
83. Why the macro would be less suitable as a general-purpose tool
Its fixed names, fixed output layout, and business-specific categories mean it is not intended for arbitrary worksheets. That is a strength in its own environment but a limitation if someone expects it to work on unknown tables.
84. Why the line-item output is easier to consume downstream
Many import or reconciliation processes prefer one record per line with a category label. That structure is simpler to filter, sum, validate, and map than a wide source table. The macro aligns the output with that downstream expectation.
85. Why the code’s clarity is more valuable than brevity
The macro repeats the same pattern across categories, but that repetition makes the business logic obvious. In financial workbook code, clarity is usually worth more than elegance alone because the code has to be trusted and maintained by humans.
86. Why the macro is likely intended for recurring use
The presence of cleanup logic, state restoration, and a versioned name suggests regular use rather than one-off execution. It is built to be refreshed repeatedly as filters change. That is a hallmark of production workbook automation.
87. Why prevScreen, prevEvents, and prevCalc are restored last
The macro only puts Excel back into its original state after the export is complete or a failure has been routed through cleanup. That ordering ensures the entire operation benefits from the performance and isolation settings during processing and then exits cleanly afterward.
88. Why Application.CutCopyMode = False is still useful
Even though the macro does not appear to use clipboard copy operations, clearing copy mode is a common tidy-up step that leaves Excel in a neutral state. It helps avoid lingering marching-ants behavior or accidental clipboard state after a macro runs.
89. Why the macro avoids using formulas in the destination
The output is written as values. That makes the import sheet stable and independent of source-table recalculation after the macro runs. A value-based output is easier to hand off and less likely to change unexpectedly.
90. Why this is a strong Excel automation pattern
It combines table awareness, filtered-row awareness, array-based processing, explicit cleanup, and controlled output shaping. Those are the ingredients of a robust workbook macro. The result is practical, readable, and aligned with the structure of the source data.
91. Why the macro can fail gracefully in many common cases
If the source is empty, if the filtered view is empty, or if required visible columns are unavailable, the macro exits without damaging the destination state beyond the intended clear. That kind of controlled failure is appropriate for an import step.
92. Why the macro is deterministic
Given the same table structure, the same visible rows, and the same source values, it will produce the same output every time. There is no randomness, external lookup, or uncertain inference. That makes it suitable for auditing and repeatable data processing.
93. Why the final destination data is trustworthy within its contract
The macro does not guess category meanings or infer missing data from unrelated fields. It uses explicit mappings and explicit sums. As long as the source table follows the expected contract, the output should be predictable and reliable.
94. Final technical assessment
This VBA macro is a filter-aware, category-expanding export routine built for a fixed workbook template. It reads visible rows from a structured source table, transforms them into normalized line-item records, and writes the result into a staging sheet. Its strengths are performance, clarity, and business-rule transparency. Its main fragility points are the fixed schema dependency and the one unqualified clear range. Within its intended workbook, it is a solid and practical solution.
95. Compact summary of the workflow
1. Save Excel state.
2. Disable screen updates, events, and automatic calculation.
3. Bind source and destination objects.
4. Find visible rows in the source table.
5. Count visible areas and size output arrays.
6. Loop through each category in compHeaders.
7. Read source values by visible area.
8. Convert amounts safely and skip zeros.
9. Write name, amount, category, and approval code into output arrays.
10. Bulk-write populated arrays to the destination sheet.
11. Restore Excel state and exit cleanly.
96. Why the macro is useful in a payroll or staffing context
The source headers point to employee compensation components, and the output format converts those components into category-based records. That makes the macro useful for payroll staging, staff-cost import, or reconciliation workflows where the source workbook contains detailed salary components that need to be repackaged for another process.
97. Why the design is intentionally conservative
The macro never tries to be clever about unknown fields. It sticks to known names, known categories, and known output columns. That conservatism reduces the chance of silent misclassification, which is exactly what you want in a workbook handling financial or HR-related data.
98. Why the export format is easier to validate than the source format
The source table is wide and mixed-purpose. The export output is narrow and standardized. That makes the output much easier to inspect line by line, especially when the same record can generate several lines.
99. Why the repeated structure is valuable for maintenance
Every category branch follows the same overall template. That means future maintainers can understand one branch and then quickly interpret the others. The code is repetitive in a useful way: it reveals the pattern instead of obscuring it.
100. Closing technical judgment
This is a disciplined VBA export transformer. It is built around one source table, one filtered subset of that table, and one destination import area. It does a real business conversion rather than a literal copy. The code is strongest where it is explicit and controlled, and weakest where it depends on fixed names and one unqualified clear range. In the intended workbook, it is a practical, efficient, and understandable implementation.
