Table 10
Technical breakdown
Option Explicit

Public Sub GrossUp_GoalSeek_PPh21()
Dim wbOrig As Workbook
Dim wsOrig As Worksheet
Dim wbTemp As Workbook
Dim wsTemp As Worksheet
Dim prevCalc As XlCalculation
Dim prevScreen As Boolean
Dim prevEvents As Boolean
Dim prevAlerts As Boolean
Dim startTime As Double
Dim elapsed As Double
Dim targetCell As String
Dim origPath As String
Dim origSheetName As String
Dim tempAL1 As Variant
Dim tempLastRow As Long

If ActiveWorkbook Is Nothing Then Exit Sub
Set wbOrig = ActiveWorkbook

If Not TypeOf wbOrig.ActiveSheet Is Worksheet Then Exit Sub
Set wsOrig = wbOrig.ActiveSheet

Select Case UCase$(Left$(wsOrig.Name, 3))
Case "JAN", "FEB", "MAR", "APR", "MAY", "JUN", _
"JUL", "AUG", "SEP", "OCT", "NOV", "DEC"
targetCell = "AL1"
Case Else
Exit Sub
End Select

If Len(wbOrig.Path) = 0 Then
MsgBox "Please save the workbook first before running this macro.", vbExclamation
Exit Sub
End If

origPath = wbOrig.FullName
origSheetName = wsOrig.Name

prevCalc = Application.Calculation
prevScreen = Application.ScreenUpdating
prevEvents = Application.EnableEvents
prevAlerts = Application.DisplayAlerts

startTime = Timer
On Error GoTo CleanUp

Application.Calculation = xlCalculationManual
Application.ScreenUpdating = False
Application.EnableEvents = False
Application.DisplayAlerts = False

' Copy only the active month sheet into a temp workbook.
wsOrig.Copy
Set wbTemp = ActiveWorkbook
Set wsTemp = wbTemp.Worksheets(1)

' Break external workbook links immediately after copy.
BreakWorkbookLinks wbTemp

' Close the original workbook so it does not participate in recalculation.
wbOrig.Close SaveChanges:=False
Set wbOrig = Nothing

' Process the temp workbook only.
ProcessGoalSeek wsTemp, "AI", "AH", "AM", "AL", "K", "AL6:AL1000"

tempAL1 = wsTemp.Range("AL1").Value2
tempLastRow = wsTemp.Cells(wsTemp.Rows.Count, wsTemp.Range("K1").Column).End(xlUp).Row
If tempLastRow < 6 Then tempLastRow = 6

' Reopen the original workbook and copy back only result columns.
Set wbOrig = Workbooks.Open(Filename:=origPath, UpdateLinks:=False, ReadOnly:=False)

On Error Resume Next
Set wsOrig = wbOrig.Worksheets(origSheetName)
On Error GoTo CleanUp

If wsOrig Is Nothing Then
Err.Raise vbObjectError + 1000, , "Original sheet not found after reopening workbook."
End If

wsOrig.Range("AL1").Value2 = tempAL1

If tempLastRow >= 6 Then
wsOrig.Range("R6:R" & tempLastRow).Value2 = wsTemp.Range("R6:R" & tempLastRow).Value2
wsOrig.Range("AL6:AL" & tempLastRow).Value2 = wsTemp.Range("AL6:AL" & tempLastRow).Value2
End If

wbOrig.Save

CleanUp:
elapsed = GetElapsedSeconds(startTime)

On Error Resume Next
If Not wsOrig Is Nothing Then
If Len(targetCell) > 0 Then wsOrig.Range(targetCell).Value2 = Round(elapsed, 2)
End If

If Not wbTemp Is Nothing Then wbTemp.Close SaveChanges:=False
If Not wbOrig Is Nothing Then wbOrig.Save

Application.Calculation = prevCalc
Application.EnableEvents = prevEvents
Application.ScreenUpdating = prevScreen
Application.DisplayAlerts = prevAlerts
On Error GoTo 0
End Sub

Private Sub BreakWorkbookLinks(ByVal wb As Workbook)
Dim links As Variant
Dim i As Long

On Error Resume Next
links = wb.LinkSources(Type:=xlLinkTypeExcelLinks)
On Error GoTo 0

If IsEmpty(links) Or IsNull(links) Then Exit Sub

On Error Resume Next
If IsArray(links) Then
For i = LBound(links) To UBound(links)
wb.BreakLink Name:=CStr(links(i)), Type:=xlLinkTypeExcelLinks
Next i
Else
wb.BreakLink Name:=CStr(links), Type:=xlLinkTypeExcelLinks
End If
On Error GoTo 0
End Sub

Private Sub ProcessGoalSeek(ws As Worksheet, _
ByVal checkCol As String, _
ByVal rValCol As String, _
ByVal formulaCol As String, _
ByVal changingCellCol As String, _
ByVal lastRowCol As String, _
ByVal clearRange As String)

Dim lastRow As Long
Dim r As Long
Dim iter As Long
Dim terVal As String
Dim oVal As String
Dim rVal As Double
Dim sVal As Double
Dim tol As Double
Dim gsErr As Long
Dim success As Boolean

Dim checkColNum As Long
Dim rValColNum As Long
Dim formulaColNum As Long
Dim changingCellColNum As Long
Dim lastRowColNum As Long

checkColNum = ws.Range(checkCol & "1").Column
rValColNum = ws.Range(rValCol & "1").Column
formulaColNum = ws.Range(formulaCol & "1").Column
changingCellColNum = ws.Range(changingCellCol & "1").Column
lastRowColNum = ws.Range(lastRowCol & "1").Column

On Error Resume Next
ws.Range(clearRange).ClearContents
On Error GoTo 0

tol = 0.000001
lastRow = ws.Cells(ws.Rows.Count, lastRowColNum).End(xlUp).Row
If lastRow < 6 Then Exit Sub

For r = 6 To lastRow
If IsNumeric(ws.Cells(r, "BD").Value2) Then
If CDbl(ws.Cells(r, "BD").Value2) <> 0 And UCase$(Trim$(CStr(ws.Cells(r, "O").Value2))) = "N" Then
If ws.Cells(r, "R").Value2 <> 0 Then
ws.Cells(r, "R").ClearContents
End If

If ws.Cells(r, "R").Value2 = 0 Then
ws.Cells(r, "O").Value2 = "Y"
ws.Calculate

ws.Cells(r, "BE").FormulaR1C1 = "=RC[-1]-RC[-8]"
ws.Calculate

ws.Cells(r, "R").Value2 = ws.Cells(r, "BE").Value2
ws.Cells(r, "BE").FormulaR1C1 = "=RC[-1]-RC[-8]"
End If
End If
End If

oVal = Trim$(UCase$(CStr(ws.Cells(r, "O").Value2)))

If InStr(1, oVal, "Y", vbTextCompare) > 0 Then
terVal = Trim$(UCase$(CStr(ws.Cells(r, checkColNum).Value2)))

If terVal = "TER A" Or terVal = "TER B" Or terVal = "TER C" Then
If IsNumeric(ws.Cells(r, rValColNum).Value2) Then
rVal = CDbl(ws.Cells(r, rValColNum).Value2)
Else
rVal = 0#
End If

If Abs(rVal) > 0 Then
If ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum).HasFormula Then
If Not ws.Cells(r, changingCellColNum).HasFormula Then
success = False
gsErr = 0
sVal = CellDoubleValue(ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum))

For iter = 1 To 5
On Error Resume Next
success = ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum).GoalSeek(Goal:=0, ChangingCell:=ws.Cells(r, changingCellColNum))
gsErr = Err.Number
Err.Clear
On Error GoTo 0

ws.Calculate
sVal = CellDoubleValue(ws.Cells(r, formulaColNum))

If Abs(sVal) <= tol Then Exit For
If gsErr <> 0 Then Exit For
Next iter

If Abs(sVal) > tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell ws, r, formulaColNum, changingCellColNum, tol
End If
End If
End If
End If
End If
Else
ws.Cells(r, changingCellColNum).Value2 = CellValueOrZero(ws.Cells(r, "AJ")) * CellValueOrZero(ws.Cells(r, rValColNum))
End If

If IsNumeric(ws.Cells(r, "BD").Value2) Then
If CDbl(ws.Cells(r, "BD").Value2) <> 0 And UCase$(Trim$(CStr(ws.Cells(r, "O").Value2))) = "Y" Then
ws.Cells(r, "O").Value2 = "N"
ws.Cells(r, "R").Value2 = CellValueOrZero(ws.Cells(r, "R")) + CellValueOrZero(ws.Cells(r, "AL")) + CellValueOrZero(ws.Cells(r, "BE"))
End If
End If
Next r
End Sub

Private Function RefineGoalSeekCell(ws As Worksheet, _
ByVal rowNum As Long, _
ByVal formulaColNum As Long, _
ByVal changingCellColNum As Long, _
ByVal tol As Double) As Boolean

Dim cellX As Range
Dim cellF As Range
Dim x0 As Double
Dim x1 As Double
Dim x2 As Double
Dim f0 As Double
Dim f1 As Double
Dim f2 As Double
Dim stepSize As Double
Dim denom As Double
Dim attempt As Long
Dim signDir As Long

Set cellX = ws.Cells(rowNum, changingCellColNum)
Set cellF = ws.Cells(rowNum, formulaColNum)

If IsNumeric(cellX.Value2) Then
x0 = CDbl(cellX.Value2)
Else
x0 = 0#
cellX.Value2 = x0
End If

ws.Calculate
f0 = CellDoubleValue(cellF)

If Abs(f0) <= tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell = True
Exit Function
End If

stepSize = Abs(x0) * 0.1
If stepSize = 0 Then stepSize = 1#

For attempt = 1 To 6
For signDir = 1 To -1 Step -2
x1 = x0 + (signDir * stepSize)
cellX.Value2 = x1
ws.Calculate
f1 = CellDoubleValue(cellF)

If Abs(f1) <= tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell = True
Exit Function
End If

denom = f1 - f0
If Abs(denom) > 0.000000000001 Then
x2 = x1 - f1 * (x1 - x0) / denom
Else
x2 = x1 - f1
End If

cellX.Value2 = x2
ws.Calculate
f2 = CellDoubleValue(cellF)

If Abs(f2) <= tol Then
RefineGoalSeekCell = True
Exit Function
End If

If Abs(f2) < Abs(f0) Then
x0 = x2
f0 = f2
Else
x0 = x1
f0 = f1
End If
Next signDir

stepSize = stepSize * 2#
Next attempt

RefineGoalSeekCell = (Abs(f0) <= tol)
End Function

Private Function CellDoubleValue(c As Range) As Double
If IsError(c.Value2) Then
CellDoubleValue = 1E+99
ElseIf IsNumeric(c.Value2) Then
CellDoubleValue = CDbl(c.Value2)
Else
CellDoubleValue = 1E+99
End If
End Function

Private Function CellValueOrZero(c As Range) As Double
If IsError(c.Value2) Then
CellValueOrZero = 0#
ElseIf Len(Trim$(CStr(c.Value2))) = 0 Then
CellValueOrZero = 0#
ElseIf IsNumeric(c.Value2) Then
CellValueOrZero = CDbl(c.Value2)
Else
CellValueOrZero = 0#
End If
End Function

Private Function GetElapsedSeconds(ByVal startTime As Double) As Double
If startTime = 0 Then
GetElapsedSeconds = 0#
ElseIf Timer < startTime Then
GetElapsedSeconds = Timer + 86400# - startTime
Else
GetElapsedSeconds = Timer - startTime
End If
End Function
1. What the macro is doing at the highest level
This VBA module is a controlled workbook-isolation solver for a monthly sheet model. It takes the currently active month sheet, copies that single sheet into a temporary workbook, breaks any workbook links inside that temporary copy, closes the original workbook so it does not participate in recalculation, runs a row-by-row GoalSeek workflow inside the temp workbook, then reopens the original workbook and copies only selected results back. The macro is not merely “running formulas.” It is intentionally separating computation from the source file so the original workbook stays protected while the solver works.
2. Why this is a workflow macro rather than a simple procedure
The code is not a single-purpose subroutine that does one isolated task. It coordinates workbook validation, sheet filtering, link breaking, temporary workbook creation, solver execution, result extraction, timing, restoration, and cleanup. Those are all separate operational concerns. The macro therefore functions as a workflow controller, not just a formula helper. That distinction matters because the code is managing Excel state, workbook objects, and numerical solving in one sequence.
3. The operational problem this macro solves
The macro appears to support a payroll or gross-up model where rows need a changing cell adjusted until a target formula is balanced. In practical terms, it is solving row-specific equations in an Excel sheet that has a fixed layout and state flags. This kind of workbook often mixes formulas, control columns, and manual result cells, so a protected isolated-solving process is valuable. The macro is built for that exact environment.
4. Why the solver is isolated into a temp workbook
Opening a copied sheet as a temporary workbook gives the macro a sandbox. That sandbox means GoalSeek and recalculation can run without the original workbook’s other sheets, formulas, event handlers, or links interfering. It also means the original file can be closed while the solver works. That isolation reduces the chance of side effects and makes the process more stable than solving directly in the source workbook.
5. Why the code starts with Option Explicit
Option Explicit forces every variable to be declared before use. That is especially important here because the macro tracks workbook references, worksheet references, temporary result cells, timing, and solver state. A typo in a variable name inside a numerical solver can cause invisible logic failures if undeclared Variants are allowed. Explicit declaration is therefore a correctness safeguard, not just a style preference.
6. The purpose of the public entry point
Public Sub GrossUp_GoalSeek_PPh21() is the user-facing entry point. It is the procedure that a button, shortcut, or other macro trigger would call. Its role is orchestration: identify the workbook, validate the sheet, build the temp environment, invoke the row processor, copy results back, and restore Excel settings. It is the controller for the whole lifecycle.
7. Why the macro checks ActiveWorkbook first
The line If ActiveWorkbook Is Nothing Then Exit Sub prevents the solver from running in an invalid Excel context. That is a simple but important guard. It protects against edge cases where Excel may not have a proper active workbook available. In a macro that opens, closes, and reopens files, those defensive checks are important.
8. Why the active sheet must be a worksheet
If Not TypeOf wbOrig.ActiveSheet Is Worksheet Then Exit Sub ensures the macro only runs when the active sheet is a real worksheet, not a chart sheet or another sheet type. This avoids invalid object assumptions later in the process. The solver depends on worksheet-style cell addressing, so this validation prevents type mismatch problems before they start.
9. Why the sheet name is checked by month prefix
The macro only proceeds if the sheet name begins with one of the three-letter month prefixes. That acts like a template gate. It tells us the code is intended only for monthly sheets, not for any arbitrary tab in the workbook. This is a strong safety measure because it prevents accidental execution on support sheets, summary tabs, or configuration sheets.
10. Why the macro assigns targetCell = "AL1" only for month sheets
The target cell is set to AL1 because that appears to be the location where runtime or status information is written back after processing. Restricting that behavior to month sheets keeps the macro from writing timing information into unrelated sheets. The month-name check and the target cell assignment work together as a gate.
11. Why non-month sheets are ignored
If the sheet name does not match a month prefix, the macro exits immediately. That means the solver is deliberately narrow in scope. It is not trying to infer where to run or adapt to every workbook state. In operational terms, that makes the code safer because it avoids touching sheets that do not follow the expected data model.
12. Why the workbook must already be saved
The macro shows a message asking the user to save the workbook first if wbOrig.Path is empty. That is necessary because the code later reopens the original workbook using its full filename path. If the workbook has never been saved, there is no reliable file path to reopen from. The save requirement is therefore structural, not optional.
13. Why the original workbook path is captured early
origPath = wbOrig.FullName is stored before the workbook is closed. That is essential because the original workbook is closed during processing and then opened again afterward. Without saving the full path in a variable first, there would be no precise way to reopen the same file later.
14. Why the original sheet name is stored early
origSheetName = wsOrig.Name is captured before the workbook is closed. After reopening the original file, the macro needs to locate the exact sheet that was processed. This variable gives the copy-back step a stable target. It is a simple but vital piece of state preservation.
15. Why the application state is saved before changing anything
The macro stores Application.Calculation, Application.ScreenUpdating, Application.EnableEvents, and Application.DisplayAlerts. These are global settings that affect the whole Excel session. Because the macro changes them for performance and safety, it must also restore them later. Capturing the previous state first makes that possible.
16. Why calculation is switched to manual
Application.Calculation = xlCalculationManual prevents Excel from recalculating the entire workbook after every cell write. That is especially important in a solver, because the macro is going to write many intermediate values and repeatedly test formula results. Manual calculation gives the macro control over when recalculation happens.
17. Why screen updating is disabled
Application.ScreenUpdating = False avoids flickering and reduces UI overhead while the solver runs. The temporary workbook may undergo many cell changes during GoalSeek and fallback solving. Redrawing the screen after each of those changes would waste time and make the process visually noisy.
18. Why events are disabled
Application.EnableEvents = False prevents event-driven code from firing while the macro is running. That matters because writing into cells in the temporary workbook could trigger sheet or workbook events such as change handlers or calculate handlers. Those events could interfere with the solver or create recursion.
19. Why display alerts are disabled
Application.DisplayAlerts = False suppresses prompts that might appear while the macro closes workbooks, breaks links, or saves files. In an automation process like this, modal dialogs are dangerous because they can interrupt the solver and require user interaction. Disabling alerts keeps the process uninterrupted.
20. Why the active sheet is copied instead of the whole workbook
wsOrig.Copy creates a new workbook containing only the active sheet. That keeps the processing environment small and focused. A full workbook copy would drag in more sheets, more formulas, and more dependencies. Copying just the relevant monthly sheet is cleaner and safer for a controlled solver.
21. What happens when the sheet is copied
After the copy, Excel makes the new workbook active. The code captures that new workbook as wbTemp and its first worksheet as wsTemp. This is the temporary sandbox where the solver runs. At this point the original workbook is still open, but the copied sheet is its own separate workbook object.
22. Why the temporary workbook is the real processing target
All subsequent calculations happen on wsTemp, not on the original sheet. That means the solver is operating on a contained copy of the data and formulas. This is especially important when formulas reference external workbooks or other sheets. The temp workbook becomes a protected calculation zone.
23. Why workbook links are broken immediately after copy
BreakWorkbookLinks wbTemp is called right after the copy is made. This strips the temporary workbook of external workbook links so it no longer depends on the original file or other linked files. The goal is to stop external dependencies from participating in recalculation.
24. Why breaking links before closing the original matters
The copy is made while the original is still open, so the temp workbook may inherit links or references that point outward. If those links are not broken before the original closes, the temp workbook can remain dependent on a source that is about to disappear. Breaking links immediately creates a clean standalone workbook.
25. What the BreakWorkbookLinks helper is responsible for
This helper checks for Excel workbook link sources, handles both array and single-value return cases, and breaks each link one by one. It is a cleanup routine, not part of the numerical model itself. Its purpose is to make the temp workbook self-contained before solving begins.
26. Why link breaking is wrapped in error suppression
Not every workbook has external links. Some workbooks have none, some have unusual link states, and some may return null or empty results. Wrapping the logic in On Error Resume Next lets the macro continue if there is nothing to break or if link discovery returns an unexpected form.
27. Why the original workbook is closed before solving
wbOrig.Close SaveChanges:=False removes the original workbook from the live recalculation environment. The comment is explicit: the original workbook should not participate in recalculation. This is one of the most important design decisions in the macro. It keeps the source file out of the solver’s dependency chain.
28. Why the temp workbook is processed after the original is closed
Once the original workbook is closed, the temp workbook becomes the only live workbook under the macro’s control. That avoids workbook-to-workbook interaction during GoalSeek and row updates. The isolated environment is then as clean as possible before the solver starts changing cells.
29. Why the row processor is called on the temp sheet only
ProcessGoalSeek wsTemp, ... makes it clear that all row-level work happens in the copied sheet. The original file is not involved at this stage. That separation is deliberate and reduces risk. It also makes debugging easier because the processing workbook is disposable.
30. Understanding the parameters passed to ProcessGoalSeek
The call ProcessGoalSeek wsTemp, "AI", "AH", "AM", "AL", "K", "AL6:AL1000" configures the solver for the specific layout of the sheet. The parameters identify the columns or ranges used for eligibility checking, numeric trigger, target formula, changing cell, last-row anchor, and the range to clear before solving. This makes the solver reusable across similar layouts.
31. Why the clearRange is cleared before processing
ws.Range(clearRange).ClearContents removes stale values from the changing-cell area. That is a reset step. It ensures that old solving results from earlier runs do not remain in the sheet and confuse the next execution. A solver should always begin from a known baseline.
32. Why lastRow is detected from the anchor column
The macro calculates the last row using the specified anchor column. In this template, that column is K. By starting from the bottom of the worksheet and moving upward, the code finds the last meaningful row efficiently. This is a standard Excel pattern when one column is consistently populated.
33. Why the loop begins at row 6
The solver starts at row 6, which implies rows 1 through 5 are reserved for headers, labels, or control fields. Beginning the loop at row 6 prevents the solver from touching the header area. That tells us the sheet structure is fixed and that row 6 is the first meaningful data row.
34. Why the macro exits if there are no usable rows
If lastRow < 6, the procedure exits immediately. That means there is no data region below the header area. This avoids wasting time or writing into empty cells when the sheet has no rows to process.
35. Why the row loop is the central engine of the macro
The loop from row 6 to last row is where the actual business logic happens. Each row is treated individually, so the solver can process one employee or one payroll line at a time. That row-by-row structure is the correct unit of work for spreadsheet-based financial calculations.
36. The macro uses two layers of logic inside each row
Inside each row, there is first a state/control phase that examines columns like BD, O, R, AJ, AL, and BE, and then a solver phase that checks TER values, numeric triggers, formula existence, and GoalSeek applicability. This suggests the row has an internal lifecycle rather than just one static formula state.
37. Why column BD is checked as a gate
The code only enters the control branch if BD is numeric and not zero. That means BD acts like an activation or eligibility flag. Rows with zero or nonnumeric values in that column are not treated as active in that control logic.
38. Why column O behaves like a status flag
The code compares O to N and Y repeatedly. That is a strong sign that O is a mode or state field. It appears to tell the macro whether the row is in a pre-processing state, a processing state, or a finalized state.
39. Why the macro clears R under certain conditions
If the row is active (BD nonzero), O = N, and R is not already zero, the code clears R. That indicates R may hold an old stored result that must be reset before recalculation. Clearing it prevents stale values from contaminating the next state transition.
40. Why the macro writes O = Y before calculating
When R is zero in the control branch, the macro sets O to Y and recalculates. This suggests Y means the row is ready for the next phase of processing. The state change probably causes formulas elsewhere in the row to respond, so the recalculation is necessary.
41. Why the code temporarily writes a formula into BE
The macro assigns ws.Cells(r, "BE").FormulaR1C1 = "=RC[-1]-RC[-8]". This turns BE into a temporary working cell that computes a difference between two nearby values in the same row. The exact business meaning depends on the sheet layout, but operationally it is a derived value used to feed the row’s stored result.
42. Why the BE formula is recalculated immediately
After writing the formula, the macro recalculates the sheet. That ensures the formula result is available before the code copies it into R. Without the recalculation, the code might transfer an old value or an uncomputed state.
43. Why R receives the value from BE
The line ws.Cells(r, "R").Value2 = ws.Cells(r, "BE").Value2 stores the computed result from the temporary formula cell into R. This suggests R is a persistent result or balance cell, while BE is a temporary calculation aid. That separation is useful because it keeps the worksheet model intact while still capturing the computed value.
44. Why the BE formula is restored after copying to R
After the value is moved into R, the macro restores the formula in BE. This is a crucial detail. It means BE remains part of the live worksheet logic rather than being permanently overwritten by a hardcoded number. The sheet retains its formula structure for future recalculations.
45. Why the solver later checks the TER value
After the control logic, the macro normalizes the value in the check column and only proceeds when it equals TER A, TER B, or TER C. That is a business-rule filter. It means only specific row categories are eligible for GoalSeek. The solver is not applied to all rows indiscriminately.
46. Why the TER comparison uses text normalization
The code uses Trim$ and UCase$ so that differences in spacing and casing do not prevent valid matches. This is defensive text handling. Spreadsheet values often contain inconsistent formatting, and the solver should not reject a row because of superficial casing differences.
47. Why the numeric trigger column is checked next
After the TER category check, the code reads the numeric trigger column and only proceeds when its absolute value is nonzero. This gives the row a second gate. A row must belong to the right category and also contain a meaningful numeric trigger before the solver will attempt GoalSeek.
48. Why the trigger is converted to Double
If the trigger value is numeric, it is converted to Double. If not, it becomes zero. This avoids type errors and makes the later absolute-value test reliable. It is a simple safety layer around user-entered or formula-generated worksheet data.
49. Why the target formula must contain a formula
The code checks HasFormula on the formula column before trying GoalSeek. That is required because GoalSeek is only meaningful when there is a formula result to drive toward zero. If the cell is a plain value, there is no equation to solve.
50. Why the changing cell must not contain a formula
The changing cell is checked to ensure it does not already have a formula. GoalSeek needs a direct input cell it can write to. If the changing cell were formula-driven, the solver would be trying to control a dependent output instead of a writable variable.
51. Why GoalSeek is the first solving strategy
Excel’s built-in GoalSeek is the most natural first step for a one-variable target problem. It is already optimized inside Excel and usually works well when the formula behaves smoothly. The code correctly uses GoalSeek before attempting a custom numerical fallback.
52. Why GoalSeek is retried multiple times
The macro allows up to five GoalSeek attempts. That is a practical choice because a row may need recalculation passes to settle. Repeating GoalSeek after recalc can improve the chance of convergence. The loop is bounded so it does not run forever.
53. Why the error number is captured after GoalSeek
Immediately after GoalSeek, the code stores Err.Number and clears the error state. That allows the macro to know whether GoalSeek failed due to a runtime issue rather than merely not reaching tolerance. It is a careful distinction between numerical failure and execution failure.
54. Why the formula value is re-read after each GoalSeek attempt
After each attempt, the sheet is recalculated and the formula residual is re-read. This tells the code whether the target is now close enough to zero. Checking the actual formula result after recalculation is the only reliable way to know whether the solver has succeeded.
55. Why the tolerance is so strict
tol = 0.000001 is a very tight tolerance. That suggests the workbook expects near-exact balance. In payroll and tax-style calculations, small residuals can matter because downstream rounding or summation can amplify small errors. The strict tolerance shows the model wants a high level of precision.
56. Why the fallback solver exists at all
GoalSeek is useful, but it is not always sufficient. Some formulas are nonlinear, flat, piecewise, or sensitive to the workbook state. The fallback exists so the macro still has another numerical strategy if GoalSeek fails. That makes the overall process more robust.
57. What the fallback function is conceptually doing
RefineGoalSeekCell is a manual one-variable root search. It starts from the current changing-cell value, calculates the residual, tests values on either side, estimates a better point using slope information, and expands the step size if needed. In spirit, it is a controlled local search with secant-like updates.
58. Why the fallback reads the current changing-cell value first
The function begins by reading the existing input cell into x0. If the cell is not numeric, it initializes it to zero. That guarantees the search starts from a valid numeric point even when the worksheet cell is blank or contains text.
59. Why the fallback recalculates immediately after seeding the starting point
After setting x0, the macro recalculates the sheet and reads the formula residual into f0. This establishes the baseline residual before any probing begins. If the baseline is already within tolerance, the function can exit immediately.
60. Why the initial step size depends on the current value
stepSize = Abs(x0) * 0.1 makes the search scale-aware. Large starting values lead to larger steps; small values lead to small steps. If the starting value is zero, the code falls back to a step size of 1. That is a practical heuristic for spreadsheet search problems.
61. Why the fallback probes in both positive and negative directions
The loop tests x0 + stepSize and x0 - stepSize. That is important because the root could lie on either side of the current guess. Probing both directions prevents directional bias and improves the chance of finding a better residual nearby.
62. Why the fallback exits immediately when a candidate is good enough
After each probe, the code checks the residual right away. If the absolute value is within tolerance, the function returns success. That early exit is important because once the solver has found a good enough answer, any additional iterations would be wasted work and could even move away from the solution.
63. Why the secant-style update is useful
If a probe improves the residual but does not solve the row, the function computes a new candidate using the slope implied by the last two points. This is similar to the secant method. It is useful because it estimates where the residual would cross zero without needing a derivative.
64. Why the denominator check matters in the fallback
The secant-style update divides by f1 - f0. If that difference is tiny, the slope estimate becomes unstable. The code therefore checks the denominator against a very small threshold. If the slope is unreliable, it falls back to a simpler correction rather than dividing by a near-zero number.
65. Why the fallback uses a simpler correction when the slope is unusable
When the denominator is too small, the code uses x1 - f1 instead of the secant formula. That is a crude but safe fallback. It keeps the solver moving instead of stalling on an unstable slope estimate. The design favors robustness over mathematical elegance.
66. Why the best-so-far residual is retained
After probing, the function compares the new residual to the current best residual and keeps whichever is better. That means the solver remembers the best candidate found so far rather than blindly replacing it with the latest trial value. This is especially important in noisy or irregular spreadsheet formulas.
67. Why the step size doubles after each outer attempt
If the current neighborhood does not contain a good solution, the code expands the search radius by doubling the step size. That lets the solver search more widely without abandoning the current point immediately. It is a common and effective heuristic for root searches in spreadsheets.
68. Why the fallback is limited to six attempts
The attempt cap keeps the search from consuming unlimited time. Every attempt involves at least several recalculations, and recalculation can be expensive. The fixed bound makes the runtime predictable and prevents the solver from hanging on difficult rows.
69. Why RefineGoalSeekCell returns a Boolean
The Boolean return value indicates whether the row ended within tolerance. Even if the caller does not currently branch on it, the design makes the function reusable and future-proof. A later version could log failures, alert the user, or take a different path based on the returned status.
70. Why CellDoubleValue returns a huge sentinel for errors
CellDoubleValue returns 1E+99 for error or nonnumeric values. That guarantees invalid values cannot be mistaken for a successful residual near zero. Using a large sentinel is a classic defensive technique in numeric search code.
71. Why CellValueOrZero is a separate helper
CellValueOrZero is different from CellDoubleValue. It returns zero for errors, blanks, and nonnumeric values because it is used in arithmetic expressions where zero is a safe default. That makes it useful when the code needs to multiply or add cells rather than interpret a residual.
72. What the hardcoded columns imply about the template
The solver uses hardcoded columns such as BD, O, R, AJ, AL, and BE inside the row loop. That implies the worksheet has a fixed layout where those columns have special meanings. The macro is therefore partially generic and partially tied to a specific template structure.
73. Why this combination of parameterized and hardcoded columns is intentional
The macro accepts parameters for the main solver columns, but it still uses fixed columns for the row-control logic. This suggests the workbook’s core control mechanism is stable while some solver positions may vary. The design balances reuse with template specificity.
74. Why column O appears to behave like a mode switch
The row logic treats O as if it indicates whether the row is in one state or another. When O = N, the macro does one sequence of operations. When O = Y, it finalizes or toggles the row back. That pattern resembles a state machine more than a simple data field.
75. Why the macro toggles O instead of using another temporary variable
Using the worksheet cell itself as the state flag means the workbook stores its own processing status. That is practical because the sheet can remember whether a row has been prepared or finalized across recalculations or partial runs. The macro is leveraging the worksheet as both data store and control store.
76. Why the control branch clears and rewrites row state
The branch that handles BD, O, R, and BE looks like a preparation phase. It resets stale content, sets a mode, recalculates, derives a temporary result, and stores it. This sequence indicates the row may need to be staged before the actual GoalSeek logic is applied.
77. Why the BE formula uses relative references
FormulaR1C1 = "=RC[-1]-RC[-8]" is written using relative references. That means the formula adapts to the current row and fixed column offset. It is a compact way to define a row-local calculation without hardcoding addresses for each row.
78. Why relative referencing is helpful in this macro
The macro processes many rows, so a relative formula is more scalable than writing a row-specific formula each time. It also mirrors the worksheet’s structure, where one row’s calculation is likely similar to the next. Relative formulas are efficient and easy to replicate.
79. Why the macro does not leave BE as a constant value
The formula in BE is restored after the value is copied into R. That is important because the worksheet likely expects BE to continue functioning as a calculation cell. Permanently overwriting it with a hardcoded value would break the sheet’s design.
80. Why the code compares BD and O more than once
The macro checks BD and O before and after the solving step. That implies those values are not just static inputs; they are part of a state transition. The final check probably confirms whether the row should be flipped back or finalized after solving.
81. Why the row may be updated even when the solver is not run
If O does not contain Y, the changing cell gets a fallback value based on AJ * R. That means the row can still be seeded or updated even when it does not enter the GoalSeek path. This makes the process more resilient because rows can be partially prepared before solving.
82. Why the AJ * R product is likely a derived seed
The value written to the changing cell in the Else branch appears to be a product of two row values. That suggests one is a multiplier or factor and the other is a base amount. The exact business meaning is workbook-specific, but operationally it is a calculated seed for the solver.
83. Why row classification and row state are separate concepts
The TER A / TER B / TER C test is a business eligibility filter, while the O and BD logic looks like a processing-state filter. These are different things. One decides whether the row belongs to the target category; the other decides how the row should be handled in the solver lifecycle.
84. Why the macro is careful about zero and nonzero values
Zero is used repeatedly as an inactivity or reset threshold. Nonzero values trigger processing. This is common in spreadsheet logic because zero can serve as a simple sentinel for “not active” or “not yet calculated.” The macro uses that convention consistently.
85. Why the code reads cell values with Value2
Using .Value2 avoids some of the date/currency conversion behavior that .Value can apply. That is useful when you want the raw numeric or text content of the cell without extra formatting interpretations. In a solver, raw values are usually the safer choice.
86. Why ws.Calculate is used instead of recalculating the whole application every time
ws.Calculate recalculates the target worksheet rather than the entire Excel application. That is more focused and potentially faster when the solver only needs the current sheet’s formulas updated. It helps keep the temporary workbook processing contained.
87. Why the solver recalculates often
Every time the code changes a state cell, a changing cell, or a temporary formula, it recalculates so it can read the new residual. Without these recalculations, the macro would be working with stale values and its decisions would be wrong. Frequent recalc is necessary in this kind of iterative Excel model.
88. Why the original workbook is reopened after the temp processing
Once the temp workbook has the processed values, the original workbook is reopened from the stored path so the results can be copied back into the source file. This design preserves the original workbook as the user’s working file while still using a temporary sandbox for the heavy computation.
89. Why the result copy-back is limited to selected columns
The macro does not copy the whole processed sheet back. It only restores AL1, R6:RlastRow, and AL6:ALlastRow. That is a strong sign that only certain cells are meant to be persistent results, while everything else in the temp workbook is disposable processing state.
90. Why copying only specific columns is safer
Copying only the result columns reduces the chance of overwriting formulas, formatting, or controls that already exist in the original workbook. It also minimizes the amount of data that must be transferred back after solving. This is a targeted result-persistence design.
91. Why AL1 is copied separately from the row ranges
AL1 is a top-level cell, likely a summary value or status marker. Because it is outside the data row block, it is copied separately from the range transfers. That keeps the header-area result distinct from the row-level result ranges.
92. Why tempLastRow is derived from the temp sheet rather than the original
The macro calculates the last row after processing the temp workbook. That makes sense because the temp workbook is the authoritative environment during the solving phase. The original workbook has been closed, so the temp sheet is the correct source for determining how many processed rows need to be copied back.
93. Why tempLastRow is forced to at least 6
If the computed last row is less than 6, the code sets it back to 6. That avoids invalid range construction when there are no data rows. It keeps the copy-back logic safe by preventing ranges like R6:R5, which would be invalid.
94. Why the original sheet is reacquired after reopening the workbook
After reopening the original file, the macro tries to get the exact sheet by name again. That is necessary because the workbook object has changed. Reacquiring the sheet ensures the copy-back writes to the same month sheet that was originally processed.
95. Why there is an explicit error if the original sheet is not found
If wsOrig cannot be set after reopening, the macro raises an error stating that the original sheet was not found. That is an important guardrail. It prevents silent failure where the macro might otherwise save the workbook without restoring the results to the correct sheet.
96. Why the macro writes the temp values back using Value2
Using .Value2 for the copy-back transfers raw values directly. That is appropriate when the macro wants to preserve the calculated results rather than formulas. It also avoids extra interpretation during the value assignment.
97. Why wbOrig.Save is called after copying results back
Once the row results and summary cell have been restored to the original workbook, the file is saved. That ensures the processed values are persisted on disk. Otherwise, reopening the workbook later would lose the copied-back results.
98. Why the cleanup block always computes elapsed time
The cleanup section always computes elapsed time from startTime. That means runtime is recorded whether the macro finished normally, exited early, or encountered an error. Runtime measurement is treated as part of the process record.
99. Why the elapsed time is written to the target cell in cleanup
The cleanup block writes the rounded elapsed time to targetCell if the original sheet object still exists. This means the sheet records how long the operation took. The result is both a user-visible metric and a simple confirmation that the macro ran.
100. Why the cleanup block is essential to correctness
Because the macro changes global Excel settings, the cleanup block is not just nice to have. It is part of the correctness contract. If calculation mode, events, screen updates, or alerts were left altered, the user’s Excel session could behave strangely after the macro ends.
101. Why the temporary workbook is closed last
The temp workbook is only closed after the original workbook has been reopened and the results copied back. That ordering preserves the processing state until the source workbook is safely updated. It avoids losing the computed results too early.
102. Why the macro saves the original workbook again at the end
After cleanup, the original workbook is saved again if it still exists. That is a final persistence step. It helps ensure that any last-minute changes, including the runtime marker, are actually recorded in the workbook on disk.
103. Why the code returns Excel settings to their prior state
Restoring Calculation, EnableEvents, ScreenUpdating, and DisplayAlerts matters because those are session-wide application settings. The macro is intentionally altering them for performance and safety, so it must return them to their previous values afterward. This makes the macro behave like a reversible transaction.
104. Why the macro is robust about missing links
BreakWorkbookLinks handles the case where there are no Excel links at all. That is important because not every workbook contains links, and the solver should not fail just because the link list is empty. The helper is designed to treat link cleanup as optional if none exist.
105. Why the code checks for IsEmpty and IsNull on link sources
Different workbooks can return link information differently. Checking both empty and null values makes the helper more tolerant of Excel’s return patterns. That reduces the chance of a false error during link cleanup.
106. Why the macro uses a left-side exact row processing model
Each row is solved independently, which is the right model for spreadsheet gross-up or payroll calculations. If one row fails to solve, the rest can still be processed. That row-level independence makes the macro more resilient than a global multi-row optimization routine would be.
107. Why the macro appears built for fixed monthly template sheets
The month-prefix gate, the hardcoded row start, the explicit columns, and the result copy-back ranges all suggest a fixed template. This is not a generic workbook automation tool. It is a specialized macro built for one known workbook structure. That specialization is a strength when the structure is stable.
108. Why fixed template specialization can be a benefit
Because the sheet layout is known, the macro can be more direct and efficient. It does not need to infer column meaning or dynamically discover every control structure. That reduces complexity and lowers the risk of unexpected behavior. In workbook automation, specialization often improves reliability.
109. Why O behaves like a row lifecycle flag
The code’s repeated transitions between N and Y imply that O is not just a data value. It appears to be a workflow flag controlling row preparation and finalization. That makes the workbook itself part of the solver’s state machine.
110. Why the row state is kept in the sheet rather than only in VBA variables
Storing state in the worksheet makes the row’s condition persistent across recalculations and partial runs. If the workbook were to recalc or if the macro were rerun, the sheet still contains its own state indicator. That is useful in a template where row status matters.
111. Why R, AL, and BE seem to hold related result pieces
The final branch combines the values in R, AL, and BE. That suggests those columns each hold part of the row’s result or adjustment. The macro is consolidating them into a final stored value. The exact semantics depend on the sheet design, but the pattern is clearly additive consolidation.
112. Why the code avoids touching rows that do not satisfy the TER condition
The TER filter protects the solver from acting on irrelevant rows. That matters because not every row in a month sheet may be part of the gross-up or tax-balancing logic. Limiting the row set reduces runtime and lowers risk.
113. Why the macro keeps a strict tolerance but also a fallback
The code wants very precise balance, but it also recognizes that Excel’s native GoalSeek may not always achieve that precision alone. The strict tolerance defines success. The fallback expands the method set. Together they make the solver both demanding and resilient.
114. Why the fallback search is local rather than global
The custom refinement function only searches near the current point and expands outward gradually. It does not attempt a global search over the entire mathematical domain. That is appropriate for spreadsheet models, where the changing cell usually has a meaningful starting estimate and local search is cheaper than exhaustive exploration.
115. Why the code never assumes exact arithmetic equality
The solver checks whether the absolute residual is less than or equal to a tolerance. It does not expect the formula cell to equal exactly zero in a strict floating-point sense. That is correct for Excel, where decimals and iterative formulas often leave tiny residuals.
116. Why the macro is careful about stale values
It clears the changing range before processing, clears R in some cases, recalculates after state changes, and restores BE after temporary formula use. These are all anti-staleness measures. They keep old values from contaminating new calculations.
117. Why the code uses comments to document major phases
The comments in the code identify the purpose of each phase: copying the active month sheet, breaking links, closing the original workbook, processing the temp workbook, and copying results back. Those comments are useful because they show the author thought about workbook lifecycle, not just cell formulas.
118. Why the cleanup block is designed to survive partial failure
Even if some step earlier in the macro fails, the cleanup logic still tries to write elapsed time, close the temp workbook, save the original workbook if it exists, and restore Excel settings. That makes the macro more durable because it aims to leave the workbook environment in a usable state.
119. What the macro is doing mathematically
Mathematically, the macro is solving one-variable target problems row by row. It uses a combination of Excel GoalSeek and a custom secant-like refinement loop. The target is effectively a zero residual in a formula cell, and the changing cell is the variable being adjusted.
120. Final technical assessment
This VBA module is a disciplined workbook-isolation solver for a structured monthly payroll or gross-up template. Its strengths are isolation, controlled recalculation, explicit state management, result copy-back discipline, and a fallback numerical solver when GoalSeek is not enough. Its main limitation is that it is clearly template-specific, with some hardcoded row-state columns and fixed assumptions about sheet structure. Within that intended model, it is a strong and practical design.
121. The row solver is really a state machine with numeric side effects
The macro does not simply test a row and solve it. It moves the row through states, writes intermediate values, recalculates, and conditionally updates persistent result cells. That makes the row logic a state machine disguised as spreadsheet automation. The numeric solver is only one part of that machine.
122. Why the state machine matters for reliability
When a workbook uses internal state flags like O, BD, and R, the macro can resume a row in the correct phase even after recalculation or partial reruns. That is more reliable than treating the sheet as stateless. The state flag gives the workbook memory.
123. Why the loop does not simply solve every row the same way
Rows are not treated uniformly because the workbook itself is not uniform. Some rows are active, some are not, some need result seeding, and some need finalization. The conditional logic exists because the model has operational stages, not just static cells.
124. Why the control branch is executed before the TER filter
The control logic affects row state regardless of whether the row will later enter GoalSeek. That means the workbook may need preparatory or cleanup updates even on rows that are not part of the balance category. This is a subtle but important sign that the TER rule and the state logic solve different problems.
125. Why the BE temporary formula is likely a bridge value
The formula in BE is created, computed, copied to R, and then restored. That strongly suggests BE is not the final answer itself but a bridge between underlying worksheet logic and a stored output. Bridge values are common in Excel models where formulas are too complex to move directly into a target cell.
126. Why the code prefers writing to sheet cells over keeping everything in memory
The workbook’s own formula network is part of the calculation. If the macro kept all values only in VBA variables, it would bypass the worksheet formulas that define the model. Writing to cells allows Excel’s calculation engine to participate in the solving process.
127. Why Excel’s calculation engine is central to this design
GoalSeek only works because Excel recalculates formulas after changing cells. The macro relies on that engine for both the control branch and the solver branch. The VBA code is therefore an orchestrator, not a replacement for the worksheet’s own computational logic.
128. Why the temporary workbook is useful even though it is later discarded
The temp workbook does not need to survive. It only needs to exist long enough to perform the solver steps safely. That makes it ideal for destructive or iterative processing. Once the important values are copied back, the temporary workbook can be closed without concern.
129. Why copying back only the results preserves workbook integrity
Because the original workbook is reopened fresh from disk, the macro avoids accumulating temporary side effects in the user’s main file. Only the solved values are restored. That keeps the original structure, formulas, and formatting intact while still updating the intended outputs.
130. Why the code is careful not to depend on user interaction after launch
After the macro starts, it should not need the user to answer prompts or manage objects manually. That is why alerts are suppressed and the temp workbook handles the solver work. This makes the macro suitable for repeatable execution on a known template.
131. Why the timing measurement is part of the user experience
The elapsed time written to AL1 gives immediate feedback that the macro ran and how long it took. That is useful both as a diagnostic and as a simple health check. If the runtime grows unexpectedly, the user has an obvious indicator on the sheet itself.
132. Why GetElapsedSeconds is separated into a helper function
Time calculation is not the core business logic, so separating it into a helper keeps the main macro easier to read. It also makes the midnight rollover handling reusable and testable on its own. That separation is small but good design.
133. Why the macro can tolerate midnight rollover
Excel’s Timer resets at midnight. The helper detects that by comparing the current timer value with the start time and adds 86400 seconds if needed. This keeps the elapsed measurement correct even if the macro runs across midnight.
134. Why the solver only targets rows with a valid formula cell
Rows without formulas in the target column are skipped because there is no root to find. That prevents meaningless GoalSeek calls and protects the workbook from nonsensical writes. This is a key structural safeguard.
135. Why the changing cell must remain formula-free
The changing cell is the variable in the row-level equation. If it already contained a formula, it would no longer be a direct variable. The macro protects that assumption before calling GoalSeek or the fallback search.
136. Why the fallback solver is intentionally simple
The fallback is not a full optimization library. It is a practical numeric repair routine suitable for workbook formulas. It uses a local search, slope estimate, and bounded iterations because that is enough for many spreadsheet models without introducing unnecessary complexity.
137. Why the code’s error handling is localized instead of global
Only specific sections use On Error Resume Next. That means the macro tries to be permissive where minor issues are acceptable, such as link detection or GoalSeek calls, but still keeps normal error flow elsewhere. This prevents the whole procedure from becoming blind to real problems.
138. Why Err.Clear is used after GoalSeek
After each GoalSeek attempt, the error state is cleared so the next iteration begins cleanly. That avoids an old error code contaminating later logic. In iterative code, error hygiene matters because leftover error state can mislead the control flow.
139. Why the success variable is still assigned even if not heavily used
The code stores the return value from GoalSeek into success, which makes the intent explicit even if the next logic mostly relies on the residual and error code. That variable documents the solver result and leaves room for later expansion such as logging or branch decisions.
140. Why the code calculates sVal before and after GoalSeek
sVal captures the current formula value, which is the actual measure of solver quality. GoalSeek might return a nominal success, but the residual still needs to be checked against tolerance. The code therefore treats the residual as the real acceptance criterion.
141. Why Abs(sVal) <= tol is the success test
This criterion is the proper one for a target-zero problem. The sign of the residual does not matter; only how close it is to zero. Using the absolute value makes the success condition symmetrical around the target.
142. Why a negative residual is not treated differently from a positive one
Both positive and negative residuals simply indicate the row is not yet solved. The solver only cares about magnitude relative to the tolerance. That is standard for zero-target numerical search.
143. Why the macro is effective for row-local equations
Each row can have its own changing cell, its own formula, and its own activation state. That fits naturally with a row-by-row GoalSeek design. The macro does not need to solve a global system because each row appears to be independent enough to handle separately.
144. Why the macro would be harder to generalize without losing safety
Hardcoded structural assumptions like month-name sheets, row starts, control columns, and output columns make the macro less generic but safer within its intended workbook. Generalizing it would require more inference and more runtime guesswork, which could reduce reliability.
145. Why the workbook’s structure is part of the contract
The code assumes the workbook has a fixed layout. That means the worksheet design, not just the VBA code, is part of the system contract. If the template changes, the macro must be reviewed accordingly. This is normal for Excel automation tied to a business template.
146. Why the original workbook is reopened instead of kept open
Closing the original workbook before solving removes it from recalculation and event participation. Reopening it later ensures the copied-back values land in a fresh, stable workbook instance. That is cleaner than trying to preserve and repair the original instance in place.
147. Why the temporary workbook can be safely thrown away
The temp workbook exists purely as an execution environment. Once the results are copied back, its only remaining purpose would be to keep intermediate formulas and temporary values. Since those are no longer needed, closing it is the correct cleanup step.
148. Why the code deliberately writes the runtime into the sheet
The runtime write is not just logging. It gives the user an in-sheet marker of execution and helps confirm the macro completed. That can be useful in a workbook that may be run repeatedly, especially if the user wants a visible sign that the latest solve succeeded.
149. Why the design is strong even though it is template-specific
Template specificity is acceptable here because the workbook appears to have a known and stable structure. The macro benefits from that specificity by keeping its logic direct, predictable, and low overhead. The result is a practical production-style tool rather than a generic but weaker one.
150. Final technical assessment
This macro is a disciplined, state-aware, isolated GoalSeek workflow for a monthly Excel template. It protects the original workbook, processes a copied sheet in a temp workbook, uses a mixed control-and-solver routine for each row, falls back to custom numeric refinement when GoalSeek is insufficient, and restores results plus timing back into the source file. Its engineering strengths are isolation, defensive checks, controlled recalculation, and cleanup discipline. Its limitations are the expected ones for a template-bound macro: it depends on fixed sheet names, fixed control columns, and a specific workbook layout. Within that scope, it is a solid and careful implementation.
