Option Explicit
Public Sub FindOpenItemsAndMatchTarget()
Dim ws As Worksheet, outWs As Worksheet
Dim lastRow As Long, r As Long
Dim colVoucher As Long, colDesc As Long, colDebit As Long, colCredit As Long
Dim targetText As String, targetAmount As Currency
Dim n As Long, i As Long
Dim vouchers() As String, descs() As String, rows() As Long
Dim amounts() As Currency
Dim debitVal As Currency, creditVal As Currency, netVal As Currency
Dim picked() As Boolean, exactFound As Boolean
Dim outRow As Long, totalAmount As Currency
Dim headerRow As Long: headerRow = 1
Set ws = ActiveSheet
colVoucher = FindHeaderColumn(ws, headerRow, "Voucher number")
colDesc = FindHeaderColumn(ws, headerRow, "Description")
colDebit = FindHeaderColumn(ws, headerRow, "Debit")
colCredit = FindHeaderColumn(ws, headerRow, "Credit")
If colVoucher = 0 Or colDesc = 0 Or colDebit = 0 Or colCredit = 0 Then
MsgBox "Required headers not found: Voucher number, Description, Debit, Credit.", vbExclamation
Exit Sub
End If
targetText = InputBox("Enter target total (IDR):", "Open items", "725112500")
If Len(Trim$(targetText)) = 0 Then Exit Sub
targetAmount = ParseAmount(targetText)
lastRow = ws.Cells(ws.Rows.Count, colVoucher).End(xlUp).Row
ReDim vouchers(1 To lastRow - headerRow)
ReDim descs(1 To lastRow - headerRow)
ReDim rows(1 To lastRow - headerRow)
ReDim amounts(1 To lastRow - headerRow)
n = 0
For r = headerRow + 1 To lastRow
debitVal = ParseAmount(ws.Cells(r, colDebit).Value)
creditVal = ParseAmount(ws.Cells(r, colCredit).Value)
netVal = Abs(debitVal - creditVal)
If IsOpenCandidate(CStr(ws.Cells(r, colDesc).Value), debitVal, creditVal) Then
If netVal > 0 Then
n = n + 1
vouchers(n) = CStr(ws.Cells(r, colVoucher).Value)
descs(n) = CStr(ws.Cells(r, colDesc).Value)
rows(n) = r
amounts(n) = netVal
End If
End If
Next r
If n = 0 Then
MsgBox "No open candidates found.", vbInformation
Exit Sub
End If
ReDim Preserve vouchers(1 To n)
ReDim Preserve descs(1 To n)
ReDim Preserve rows(1 To n)
ReDim Preserve amounts(1 To n)
QuickSortCandidates amounts, vouchers, descs, rows, 1, n
ReDim picked(1 To n)
exactFound = FindSubset(amounts, picked, 1, 0, targetAmount, n)
On Error Resume Next
Application.DisplayAlerts = False
ThisWorkbook.Worksheets("Open Items").Delete
Application.DisplayAlerts = True
On Error GoTo 0
Set outWs = ThisWorkbook.Worksheets.Add
outWs.Name = "Open Items"
outWs.Range("A1:E1").Value = Array("Voucher number", "Source Row", "Amount", "Description", "Status")
outRow = 2
totalAmount = 0
If exactFound Then
For i = 1 To n
If picked(i) Then
outWs.Cells(outRow, 1).Value = vouchers(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 2).Value = rows(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 3).Value = amounts(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 4).Value = descs(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 5).Value = "Selected"
totalAmount = totalAmount + amounts(i)
outRow = outRow + 1
End If
Next i
outWs.Cells(outRow + 1, 1).Value = "Total"
outWs.Cells(outRow + 1, 3).Value = totalAmount
outWs.Cells(outRow + 1, 5).Value = "Matched target"
Else
For i = 1 To n
outWs.Cells(outRow, 1).Value = vouchers(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 2).Value = rows(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 3).Value = amounts(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 4).Value = descs(i)
outWs.Cells(outRow, 5).Value = "Candidate"
totalAmount = totalAmount + amounts(i)
outRow = outRow + 1
Next i
outWs.Cells(outRow + 1, 1).Value = "Total of candidates"
outWs.Cells(outRow + 1, 3).Value = totalAmount
outWs.Cells(outRow + 1, 5).Value = "No exact match found"
End If
outWs.Columns("A:E").AutoFit
MsgBox "Done. Check the 'Open Items' sheet.", vbInformation
End Sub
Private Function IsOpenCandidate(ByVal desc As String, ByVal debitVal As Currency, ByVal creditVal As Currency) As Boolean
Dim s As String
s = LCase$(Trim$(desc))
If Abs(debitVal - creditVal) <= 0 Then
IsOpenCandidate = False
Exit Function
End If
If ContainsAny(s, Array( _
"reversal", "reverse", "correct", "correction", "cancel", "cancelling", _
"migrate", "reclass", "adjust", "reverting", "correcting", _
"settlement", "pertanggung", "refund", "pengembalian", "over refund", _
"less of payment", "bounce back")) Then
IsOpenCandidate = False
Exit Function
End If
If debitVal > 0 Then
IsOpenCandidate = True
Else
IsOpenCandidate = False
End If
End Function
Private Function ContainsAny(ByVal sourceText As String, ByVal terms As Variant) As Boolean
Dim i As Long
For i = LBound(terms) To UBound(terms)
If InStr(1, sourceText, CStr(terms(i)), vbTextCompare) > 0 Then
ContainsAny = True
Exit Function
End If
Next i
End Function
Private Function FindHeaderColumn(ByVal ws As Worksheet, ByVal headerRow As Long, ByVal headerText As String) As Long
Dim lastCol As Long, c As Long
lastCol = ws.Cells(headerRow, ws.Columns.Count).End(xlToLeft).Column
For c = 1 To lastCol
If LCase$(Trim$(CStr(ws.Cells(headerRow, c).Value))) = LCase$(Trim$(headerText)) Then
FindHeaderColumn = c
Exit Function
End If
Next c
End Function
Private Function ParseAmount(ByVal v As Variant) As Currency
Dim s As String
If IsError(v) Or IsEmpty(v) Or Len(Trim$(CStr(v))) = 0 Then
ParseAmount = 0
Exit Function
End If
If IsNumeric(v) Then
ParseAmount = CCur(v)
Exit Function
End If
s = CStr(v)
s = Replace(s, "IDR", "", , , vbTextCompare)
s = Replace(s, ",", "")
s = Replace(s, " ", "")
s = Replace(s, ChrW(160), "")
s = KeepNumericChars(s)
If Len(s) = 0 Or s = "-" Then
ParseAmount = 0
Else
ParseAmount = CCur(Val(s))
End If
End Function
Private Function KeepNumericChars(ByVal s As String) As String
Dim i As Long, ch As String, out As String
For i = 1 To Len(s)
ch = Mid$(s, i, 1)
If (ch >= "0" And ch <= "9") Or ch = "." Or ch = "-" Then
out = out & ch
End If
Next i
KeepNumericChars = out
End Function
Private Sub QuickSortCandidates(ByRef amounts() As Currency, _
ByRef vouchers() As String, _
ByRef descs() As String, _
ByRef rows() As Long, _
ByVal lo As Long, _
ByVal hi As Long)
Dim i As Long, j As Long
Dim pivot As Currency
i = lo
j = hi
pivot = amounts((lo + hi) \ 2)
Do While i <= j
Do While amounts(i) > pivot
i = i + 1
Loop
Do While amounts(j) < pivot
j = j - 1
VBA code overview
This macro is a target-matching open-items solver. It scans the active worksheet, identifies rows that look like unresolved accounting items, normalizes their debit and credit values into a monetary amount, filters out rows that are unlikely to belong to the open-item pool, sorts the remaining candidates by size, and then performs a recursive subset search to find a combination of rows whose total equals a user-provided target amount exactly.

If an exact combination is found, the macro creates a fresh sheet called Open Items and writes only the selected rows. If no exact combination is found, it still writes all candidates so the user can review them manually. That makes the macro both a solver and a reporting tool.
1. What this macro is solving in business terms
The macro is solving a reconciliation problem. In many finance and accounting workflows, there is a target amount that needs to be explained by a group of open voucher lines. The user does not want a random list of rows. The user wants the rows that collectively match a balance. This macro attempts to find those rows automatically.

That is why the code is built around a target input, candidate filtering, sorting, recursion, and output reconstruction. It is not a generic filter or a simple sorter. It is a practical accounting matching engine.
2. Why Option Explicit is important here
Option Explicit requires every variable to be declared before it is used. In a macro that uses arrays, counters, row numbers, worksheet references, amounts, and Boolean flags, that matters a great deal. A single typo in a variable name could otherwise create a new Variant silently and break the logic in a way that is hard to detect.

In a search routine that relies on exact totals, silent variable errors are especially dangerous because they can produce wrong results without raising an obvious failure. Option Explicit helps prevent that class of bug.
3. The public procedure as the workflow controller
FindOpenItemsAndMatchTarget is the main entry point. It coordinates the entire workflow rather than doing every task itself. It identifies the source columns, prompts the user for the target amount, scans the worksheet, builds a candidate set, sorts it, performs the subset search, rebuilds the output sheet, and writes the result.

That division is a good design choice because it keeps the macro readable. The helper functions do focused tasks, while the main procedure shows the overall execution flow.
4. Why the macro works on the active sheet
Set ws = ActiveSheet means the macro assumes the user has already selected the correct input worksheet before running it. That makes the user experience simple because the macro can be launched directly from the sheet being reconciled. There is no need to choose a sheet in code or browse through workbook objects.

The tradeoff is that the macro depends on the user being on the correct worksheet. It is designed for convenience in a controlled workbook, not for autonomous multi-sheet discovery.
5. Header-based column detection instead of hardcoded positions
The macro searches the first row for the headers Voucher number, Description, Debit, and Credit. It does not assume those fields are in fixed column letters. That makes it more flexible when the worksheet layout changes, because the macro can still work if the columns move around but the header names remain the same.

This is a strong design choice for a workbook that may be edited or exported from different sources. It reduces fragility compared with hardcoding column positions.
6. Why the header row is fixed to row 1
headerRow = 1 means the macro expects the titles to appear in the first row. This is a common structure for accounting exports and makes the search for columns simple and fast. The macro does not attempt to detect arbitrary header placement because that would add complexity and uncertainty.

The assumption is reasonable as long as the workbook template remains stable. If the headers are not on row 1, the macro will not read the structure correctly.
7. FindHeaderColumn as the schema discovery helper
This helper loops through the used columns in the header row and compares each cell to the target header name after trimming and case-normalizing. If it finds an exact text match, it returns the column number. If it does not, it returns zero.

This function is small, but it is central to the macro because all later row scanning depends on these column indexes being correct. If the header lookup fails, the rest of the workflow cannot proceed safely.
8. Why the macro exits if a required header is missing
Before doing anything else, the macro confirms that all four required headers are present. If any are missing, it shows a message and exits. That is the right behavior because the rest of the logic would be unreliable without the right fields.

Failing early is better than continuing with the wrong schema and producing a misleading output sheet. The error message is a safeguard against false results.
9. The target amount prompt
The macro asks the user to enter a target total using an InputBox. The default value is 725112500, which gives the user a starting point but does not force that exact value. The text entered by the user is then parsed into a numeric monetary amount.

This makes the macro reusable for different reconciliation runs. The user can test one target, then another, without changing the code.
10. Why the target is taken as text first
Users often type currency values with commas, spaces, or labels such as IDR. Accepting the target as text gives the macro a chance to normalize that input before conversion. That is much more forgiving than demanding a raw numeric value and is suitable for accounting users who often paste formatted numbers.

The cleanup work is handled in the parsing helper rather than in the input prompt itself.
11. Why Currency is used for amounts
Currency is a good choice for accounting values in VBA because it is fixed-point and avoids the rounding issues that floating-point values can introduce. Since the macro looks for exact matches, numerical stability matters. If amounts were held as Double, small precision artifacts could interfere with comparisons.

Using Currency makes the exact equality test more reliable and more appropriate for money.
12. ParseAmount as the monetary normalization layer
This function handles several possible value types: blank cells, error values, numeric values already stored as numbers, and text values containing a monetary representation. It returns zero for empty or invalid values, converts numeric input directly, and cleans text input before converting it into Currency.

This helper is one of the key reasons the macro is resilient to messy worksheet data.
13. Why the parser removes IDR, commas, spaces, and nonbreaking spaces
Those are all common formatting artifacts in worksheets or pasted accounting data. By removing them, the macro turns strings such as IDR 725,112,500 into a form that can be interpreted as a number. This makes the macro tolerant of the way users actually enter values, not only the ideal case.

The parser is not trying to be a universal international number reader. It is trying to be practical for this workbook’s formatting style.
14. What KeepNumericChars does
This helper goes through the string character by character and keeps only digits, the decimal point, and the minus sign. Everything else is removed. The output is a simplified numeric string that can be passed to Val.

That is a simple but effective cleanup technique in VBA. It is especially useful when values come from a sheet where formatting and text labels may be mixed together.
15. Why the macro uses Val after cleanup
After stripping out formatting characters, the macro uses Val to convert the remaining string into a number. Val is permissive enough to work well with simple numeric strings, which is exactly what the cleanup step produces.

The code is therefore using a two-step approach: sanitize first, convert second. That is a practical pattern for accounting values in VBA.
16. What the parser does not try to solve
The parser does not attempt to handle every locale convention or every possible currency notation. It does not parse all possible negative formats or accounting-style parentheses in a sophisticated way. That means the macro is best suited to a controlled workbook environment with predictable formatting.

This is a sensible boundary for a workbook-specific tool. The goal is operational reliability, not universal parsing support.
17. Scanning the data rows
After the target is parsed, the macro finds the last used row in the voucher column and scans each row beneath the header. For every row, it reads the debit and credit amounts, computes the absolute difference, and applies the business-rule filter to decide whether the row belongs to the open-item pool.

This is the candidate-building phase. It turns worksheet rows into an in-memory list that can be sorted and searched.
18. Why the last row is found using the voucher column
The voucher column is treated as the anchor column for detecting the range of data. That suggests every meaningful row should have a voucher number. Using one anchor column is common in VBA because it is simple and efficient.

The limitation is that if there are blanks or interrupted rows in that column, the last-row detection may stop too early. In a well-structured accounting table, that is usually acceptable.
19. Why the macro computes Abs(debitVal - creditVal)
The macro is interested in the magnitude of the open balance, not the direction of the imbalance. The absolute difference makes the amount positive whether debit or credit is larger. That gives the subset search a consistent positive value to work with.

This is useful for target matching because the search only needs the size of each candidate contribution.
20. IsOpenCandidate as the main business-rule gate
This function decides whether a row should be treated as a candidate open item. It excludes zero-net rows, rejects descriptions that indicate reversal, correction, settlement, refund, or reclassification, and then only accepts the row if the debit amount is positive. This makes the candidate pool much more realistic.

It is not merely a data filter. It is a domain-specific rule set encoded in VBA.
21. Why zero-net rows are excluded immediately
If debit and credit are equal, the row is already balanced and does not help explain the target amount. Keeping zero-value rows would only add noise and make the subset search larger than necessary. The macro therefore discards them as soon as they are recognized.

That is both a logical and performance-oriented decision.
22. Why description filtering matters
The description field often contains the only human-readable hint about the type of accounting row. Terms like reversal, correct, settlement, and reclass usually suggest that the row is not an ordinary open balance. The macro uses those words as an exclusion signal to avoid including rows that are likely already closed or adjusted.

This is a practical strategy for accounting data where the status is not always captured in a formal flag column.
23. Why the exclusion list is broad and multilingual
The keyword list includes both English and Indonesian terms such as pertanggung and pengembalian. That suggests the workbook may contain mixed-language descriptions. By using a broader list, the macro catches more correction-style rows and reduces the chance that the search pool is polluted by non-open items.

It is heuristic rather than perfect, but it is realistic for spreadsheet data.
24. ContainsAny as a reusable text search helper
This function loops through a list of forbidden terms and checks whether any of them appear in the description text using case-insensitive matching. If one match is found, it returns True and the row is excluded.

Keeping the keyword logic in a helper makes the candidate function more readable and gives a single place to adjust the exclusion vocabulary later.
25. Why description normalization is necessary
The description is trimmed and converted to lowercase before the keyword check. That avoids false negatives caused by capitalization or spacing differences. Real-world worksheets often contain inconsistent text formatting even when the meaning is the same.

Normalization makes the filter more robust and less sensitive to superficial variation.
26. Why the macro requires debitVal > 0
After the other tests, the macro only accepts rows with a positive debit value. That means it is targeting a specific open-item population rather than every possible imbalance. This is a business rule that likely reflects the structure of the source accounting data.

It is not a mathematical necessity. It is a practical design choice based on the expected workbook behavior.
27. Why the candidate rows are stored in arrays
Once a row passes the filter, the macro stores its voucher number, description, source row number, and amount in parallel arrays. That is efficient because the later subset search operates entirely on these in-memory arrays rather than repeatedly reading worksheet cells.

Arrays are also easy to sort and easy to pass by reference in recursive routines.
28. Why the arrays are preallocated first
The macro dimensions the arrays up front based on the maximum possible number of data rows. That avoids repeated resizing inside the loop. After collection, the arrays are shrunk to the actual number of candidates using ReDim Preserve.

This is a standard VBA performance pattern when the final count is not known in advance.
29. Why ReDim Preserve is used after the scan
After the candidate collection is finished, the arrays may contain unused trailing positions. ReDim Preserve trims those away so the sort and search functions only work with real data. That makes the later logic cleaner and reduces unnecessary processing.

It also makes the array boundaries reflect the actual candidate count.
30. The role of rows() in traceability
The source row number is kept alongside each candidate so the output can point back to the exact source row. That matters in accounting because the result must be auditable. A selected item should not be just a number in a summary list. It should be traceable to the original worksheet line.

This is especially useful if someone later wants to verify why a specific voucher was selected.
31. Why the macro sorts candidates before searching
After collecting the candidate set, the macro sorts it in descending order by amount. This is helpful because large amounts can move the running total toward the target quickly. If a branch overshoots the target, the recursion can stop early and backtrack.

Sorting also improves the manual review experience if the search fails. The largest items appear first in the fallback candidate list.
32. Why descending order is chosen
The macro does not sort from smallest to largest. It sorts from largest to smallest because bigger items are more informative in a target-sum search. They can quickly approach the target and reduce the number of recursive branches that need to be explored.

This is a heuristic performance choice rather than a correctness requirement.
33. QuickSortCandidates as the sorting engine
This routine performs an in-place quicksort on the parallel arrays. Whenever it swaps two amounts, it also swaps the matching voucher, description, and row values. That keeps each candidate row intact as a single logical unit.

Without synchronized swaps, the output would no longer match the source rows correctly.
34. Why the sort is in-place
The macro does not create a second copy of the data for sorting. It reorders the arrays directly. That is efficient and typical for VBA. The parallel swap helpers make the in-place sort safe enough for this use case.

For a workbook-level dataset, this is a good balance between simplicity and performance.
35. Why the pivot is selected from the middle
The quicksort chooses the pivot from the middle of the active range. That is a common strategy because it often performs reasonably well on partially ordered input. The algorithm divides the array into values above and below the pivot and recursively sorts both sides.

This is standard quicksort behavior adapted to VBA arrays.
36. Why synchronized movement of metadata is essential
Each candidate row is not just a number. It is a row identity, a description, a source row number, and an amount. Those four pieces must travel together. If they get separated during sorting, the output becomes unreliable because the amount would no longer belong to the right voucher or description.

The swap helpers exist to prevent that failure.
37. The subset search is the computational heart of the macro
Once the candidates are sorted, the macro creates a Boolean picked() array and calls FindSubset. This is the actual solver. It tries to determine whether some combination of candidate rows adds exactly to the target amount.

At this point the logic moves from filtering and ordering into recursive search.
38. FindSubset as a recursive backtracking solver
The function tries two choices for each candidate: include it in the running total or exclude it. That creates a binary search tree of possibilities. The function explores the tree depth-first until it finds a matching sum or runs out of combinations.

This is the classic subset-sum pattern. It is easy to understand, though potentially expensive when many candidates remain after filtering.
39. Why exact equality is the success condition
The function only succeeds when currentSum = targetSum. It does not accept a close match. That is appropriate in accounting reconciliation because the expected outcome is often a precise tie. Using Currency helps make that exact comparison practical.

If the target is not found exactly, the macro does not guess or approximate.
40. Why currentSum > targetSum triggers pruning
As soon as the running total exceeds the target, the branch is abandoned. Because all candidate amounts are positive in the search set, there is no point in exploring that branch further. This simple pruning rule saves a large amount of time in practice.

Without it, the search would waste time on impossible combinations.
41. Why the search is depth-first
The macro commits to one branch before trying the other. That is a depth-first traversal. Depth-first search is natural for recursion and does not require storing the entire search tree in memory. In VBA, it is also simpler to write than a breadth-first solver.

The tradeoff is that worst-case search can still be expensive.
42. What picked() stores
picked() is the result map for the recursion. It records whether each candidate item belongs in the successful combination. When the recursive search returns, the output routine reads this array to know which rows to write into the final report.

This separation keeps the search function focused on decision-making rather than formatting.
43. Why recursion is appropriate here
The inclusion/exclusion decision for each row maps naturally to recursive logic. Each level of the search tree asks whether one more item should be included. That is a clean expression of the subset-sum problem, and it keeps the code shorter and easier to follow than a large manual loop structure would.

For moderate candidate counts, recursion is a reasonable and practical choice in VBA.
44. The worst-case complexity of the search
Subset sum is exponential in the worst case. Every candidate roughly doubles the number of combinations that may need to be considered. The sort and pruning rules help, but they do not change the underlying complexity class. That is the main computational limitation of this macro.

It works best when the candidate filter has already reduced the pool to a manageable size.
45. Why the macro is still practical for real accounting use
In many reconciliation scenarios, the candidate set after filtering is not enormous. The description exclusions remove a lot of rows, and the zero-difference test removes more. That means the recursive search often operates on a smaller subset than the full worksheet would suggest.

That is why a straightforward backtracking solution can be useful in practice.
46. Why the code uses exactFound separately
The recursive function returns a Boolean indicating whether a match was found. The main procedure stores that value in exactFound and then uses it to decide how the output sheet should be populated. That keeps the logic clean: the search decides, and the report displays the result.

This separation improves readability and makes the workflow easier to maintain.
47. Why the macro deletes the old Open Items sheet
Before writing new results, the macro removes any previous sheet with the same name. That ensures old data does not remain and mix with the new output. A fresh result sheet is safer than trying to update an old one, especially when the target amount changes from run to run.

This is a strong design choice for repeatable reporting.
48. Why DisplayAlerts is turned off briefly
Deleting a worksheet normally triggers a confirmation prompt. The macro suppresses that prompt so it can remove and recreate the output sheet automatically. It then restores DisplayAlerts immediately afterward. That is important because leaving alerts off would affect the rest of the Excel session in an unintended way.

The suppression is scoped only to the deletion step.
49. Why the sheet deletion is wrapped in error suppression
If the Open Items sheet does not exist, deletion would raise an error. The macro ignores that because the absence of the sheet is not a problem. This is a sensible use of On Error Resume Next for a noncritical cleanup step.

The intent is to make the output reset automatic, not fragile.
50. Why the output sheet is recreated rather than cleared
Creating a new sheet guarantees a blank output area with no hidden formatting, merged cells, or leftover content. Clearing an existing sheet would not fully remove all state. Recreating the sheet is cleaner and more predictable.

For a repeated reporting macro, that is usually the safer approach.
51. The output headers and what they mean
The macro writes five columns: Voucher number, Source Row, Amount, Description, and Status. These columns are enough to identify the selected item, trace it back to the original data, and understand whether the row is part of the exact match or just part of the fallback candidate list.

This output design is practical because it supports both auditability and manual follow-up.
52. Why Status is included
Status shows whether a row was Selected in an exact match or merely listed as a Candidate when no exact match was found. That helps the user immediately understand the solver outcome. The sheet is therefore not just a data dump. It is a decision report.

That small label makes the result much easier to read quickly.
53. How the exact-match branch writes the result
If the recursive search finds a valid subset, the macro writes only the rows marked True in the picked() array. It labels each one Selected and adds its amount to a running total. At the end, it writes a summary row showing the total and marks it as matched to the target.

This creates a compact reconciliation output focused only on the successful combination.
54. How the no-match branch writes the result
If no exact subset is found, the macro writes all candidate rows to the output sheet and labels each one Candidate. It then writes a total row for the full candidate set and marks the status as No exact match found. That way the user still gets a useful list instead of a blank or failed report.

This fallback behavior is important because the macro remains informative even when the exact target cannot be reached.
55. Why the macro always produces a visible result
The routine is designed so the user is not left with nothing. If an exact subset exists, it is shown. If not, the candidate list is shown. That makes the macro useful in both success and failure cases. It never ends as a silent black box with no output.

That is a very practical choice for finance users.
56. Why the source row number is included in the report
The source row lets the user go back to the original worksheet and verify the line item. In accounting work, the ability to trace a selected item back to the source data is essential. A report that cannot be traced is much less useful, even if the total is correct.

This makes the output auditable and review-friendly.
57. Why AutoFit is called at the end
The macro auto-fits the output columns so the results are readable without manual resizing. That does not affect the algorithm, but it improves the usability of the finished report. The user can immediately inspect the sheet after the macro completes.

It is a small finishing step, but it makes the output more polished.
58. Why the completion message matters
The final message confirms that the macro has finished and tells the user to check the Open Items sheet. That is useful because a workbook with many sheets can otherwise leave the user uncertain about where the result appeared.

The message is simple, but it closes the loop on the workflow.
59. Why the macro is deterministic
Given the same source data and the same target amount, the macro should produce the same output every time. There is no randomness in the sort, filter, or recursive search. That consistency is important because reconciliation work needs repeatable results that can be checked again later.

Determinism also makes the code easier to test and support.
60. Why candidate filtering is also a performance strategy
Every row excluded by IsOpenCandidate reduces the size of the search tree. That means the business rules are not only correcting the candidate list. They are also making the recursion computationally feasible. The filter and the solver are therefore connected both logically and operationally.

The better the filter, the smaller and faster the search.
61. Why heuristics are acceptable in this context
The macro uses keywords and debit-side logic to infer which rows are likely open items. That is a heuristic, not a perfect semantic classification. But in accounting spreadsheets, a practical heuristic is often enough to reduce the search space meaningfully while still leaving room for manual review.

The code is not claiming to fully understand every row. It is making a useful operational judgment.
62. What the macro assumes about the source data
The source workbook is assumed to have one row per accounting line item, a stable header row, a voucher number, a description, and debit/credit amounts. It is also assumed that the rows of interest are debit-positive open items and that correction-style entries should be excluded. Those assumptions are what make the algorithm manageable in VBA.

The macro is built for a known template, not a fully arbitrary spreadsheet.
63. Why this macro is more than a filter
At first glance, it may look like a list builder. In reality, it is a solver. It does not just say which rows match a condition. It tries to find a combination of rows that together equal a target amount. That is a much more computationally meaningful task.

The recursive search is what makes it a true matching engine.
64. The role of totalAmount in reporting
totalAmount is used only for the output sheet. It is not part of the recursion itself. The search function decides the subset, and the report logic computes and displays the sum of whatever was selected. That separation keeps algorithmic state and presentation state distinct.

That makes the code cleaner and the output easier to verify.
65. Why the macro relies on arrays instead of worksheet access during search
Worksheet access inside a recursive solver would be slow. By moving the candidate data into arrays first, the macro keeps the search in memory, which is much faster. This is a standard VBA optimization and one of the reasons the macro is practical at all.

Arrays reduce the overhead of repeated cell reads during recursion.
66. Why the macro is row-local rather than relational
The macro treats each worksheet row as one candidate item. It does not try to interpret cross-sheet joins or multi-table dependencies. That is appropriate because accounting open items are naturally row-level entities in many workbook designs.

Row-local logic keeps the solver simpler and more transparent.
67. Why the output is auditable
The output sheet preserves the voucher number, source row, description, and amount. That is enough information for a reviewer to trace the output back to the original source and confirm why a row was selected. In financial workflows, that traceability is just as important as the matched total itself.

The macro does not hide the chosen items. It exposes them clearly.
68. Why the macro remains useful even when the exact match is absent
If the solver cannot find a perfect combination, the candidate list is still written to the result sheet. That gives the user something actionable. They can inspect the rows manually, adjust the target, or investigate why the exact match did not exist. The macro therefore remains useful even in a failure case.

That is much better than returning nothing.
69. The strongest technical point in the code
The strongest part of the macro is the combination of data cleaning, candidate filtering, and recursive exact matching. Each stage prepares the data for the next one. The result is a controlled pipeline that behaves like a practical solver rather than a loose collection of worksheet operations.

That gives the code both clarity and function.
70. The biggest technical limitation
The biggest limitation is the exponential worst-case cost of subset search. If too many candidate rows survive the filter, the recursive branching can become expensive. The sort and pruning help, but they do not change the underlying complexity class.

This means the macro is best used where the candidate set is moderate and reasonably clean.
71. Why exact matching can be too strict in some cases
The macro only accepts a perfect match. If the true operational need is an approximate tie or a near-match with rounding tolerance, this macro will not report success. That is fine for strict reconciliation, but it does make the tool all-or-nothing by design.

That strictness is useful when exact settlement is required.
72. Why the source headers are critical
The macro depends on the exact names of the headers. If the workbook uses slightly different labels, the macro stops. This rigidity protects against misreading wrong columns, but it also limits portability across different file templates.

That is a normal tradeoff for a workbook-specific automation tool.
73. Why the macro can be called a solver
A solver searches for inputs meeting a target condition. This macro does exactly that by searching combinations of rows whose total equals a target amount. The recursive matching is not an incidental feature. It is the core identity of the code.

That is why it is better described as a reconciliation solver than just a macro.
74. Why this is a good fit for finance teams
Finance users often need to quickly determine whether a group of open items can explain a balance. This macro automates that process and produces a report that can be checked immediately. That saves manual trial-and-error work and reduces the risk of human selection mistakes.

The tool is especially useful where the source data and templates are stable.
75. Why the result sheet name is meaningful
Open Items clearly describes the purpose of the output. That matters because workbook users may need to find the result quickly among many support sheets. A clear sheet name makes the macro easier to use repeatedly.

The output sheet is not meant to be hidden or mysterious. It is meant to be operational.
76. Why the macro resets output state each time
Recreating the output sheet ensures that the result reflects only the current run. It prevents old data from contaminating the new report and removes the need to manually clear previous results. That is safer and more repeatable than updating an existing sheet in place.

Fresh output is especially important in a repeated reconciliation process.
77. Why the code is modular
The code is split into helpers for column finding, amount parsing, keyword checking, sorting, swapping, and recursive search. That modularity improves readability and gives each part one clear responsibility. It also makes the code easier to maintain if the workbook rules change later.

Modularity is especially valuable in VBA, where large monolithic macros can become hard to debug.
78. Why the helper functions are short and focused
Each helper performs one simple role. FindHeaderColumn locates a column, ParseAmount normalizes a value, IsOpenCandidate applies business filters, QuickSortCandidates orders the data, and FindSubset solves the combination problem. That clarity is one reason the macro remains understandable despite being fairly substantial.

Short helpers also make troubleshooting easier.
79. Why the search logic and the output logic are separate
The recursive search decides which rows belong to the subset. The output logic writes those rows to a new sheet. Those are different tasks and should remain separate. That separation keeps the solver pure and the reporting phase easy to follow.

It also makes it easier to adjust the output format without touching the search algorithm.
80. Why the code avoids overengineering
The macro does not use memoization, dynamic programming, or advanced optimization heuristics. It uses a straightforward recursive search because the workbook context likely does not require a more complex algorithm. In a finance workbook, simplicity and maintainability often matter more than theoretical elegance.

That restraint is a strength, not a weakness.
81. Why the macro’s value depends on the filter quality
The search becomes feasible only if the candidate set is reduced enough. That makes the filter stage extremely important. The better the description and debit logic identifies true open items, the smaller the search tree becomes. In that sense, the business rules are part of the algorithm’s performance strategy as much as its accounting logic.

Good filtering is what makes brute-force matching workable.
82. Why the macro assumes positive candidate amounts
The pruning rule currentSum > targetSum assumes the amounts in the search pool are nonnegative. If negative amounts were allowed, the search logic would need to be more sophisticated. The current design therefore assumes a positive-only candidate pool, which matches many open-item workflows.

That assumption greatly simplifies the recursive search.
83. Why the macro does not attempt near-match ranking
The code does not try to return the closest possible combination if no exact match exists. It is not trying to optimize residual error. That keeps the logic simple and avoids ambiguity about what “close enough” means. For strict reconciliation, a yes/no answer is often preferable.

The tradeoff is that near-miss combinations are not surfaced automatically.
84. Why exact equality is easier to interpret
An exact match gives a clear result: the selected rows reconcile to the target or they do not. That binary outcome is easy to understand and easy to explain. In finance workflows, that simplicity is often valuable because users need direct answers rather than fuzzy ones.

The macro therefore prioritizes clarity of outcome over approximation.
85. What the source data likely looks like
The source sheet likely contains voucher lines with a voucher number, a description, a debit amount, and a credit amount. It probably represents open accounting items or settlement candidates. The macro is designed to process this kind of row-based accounting export directly in Excel.

The output sheet then becomes a working list for reconciliation or review.
86. Why the candidate description text matters so much
The description field is used to exclude rows that are likely corrections, settlements, reversals, or reclasses. That suggests the source data does not always contain a dedicated status field. The macro compensates by reading the text description as a clue to accounting meaning.

This is a common real-world pattern in spreadsheet-based finance work.
87. Why the macro is a good example of defensive VBA
It validates headers, normalizes monetary values, excludes suspicious rows, ignores zero balances, creates fresh output, restores alert behavior, and provides a clear completion message. Those are all signs of code written with real workbook behavior in mind rather than idealized input.

That defensive posture is one of the strongest qualities of the implementation.
88. Why the code is easy to extend
If the workbook later needs additional exclusion keywords, a different output column, or a different target input rule, those changes can be made in localized places. Because the logic is modular, the macro is easier to evolve than a large one-block procedure would be.

This supports longer-term maintenance in a finance environment where workbook rules can change.
89. Why the macro avoids unnecessary complexity
The code stays within a straightforward VBA pattern. It uses arrays, simple parsing, quicksort, recursion, and worksheet output. It does not bring in external dependencies or complicated abstractions. That makes it easier to trust and easier to support on a normal Excel installation.

For a workbook automation tool, that simplicity is often exactly what is needed.
90. What the macro is not trying to do
It is not trying to infer intent from every possible accounting situation. It is not doing fuzzy matching, cross-sheet reconciliation, or approximate optimization. It is focused on one task: exact target matching among filtered open-item candidates.

That narrow scope is part of why the code remains usable and readable.
91. Final execution flow from start to finish
1. Bind the active sheet.
2. Find the required header columns.
3. Exit if any header is missing.
4. Prompt the user for the target amount.
5. Normalize the target into Currency.
6. Determine the last data row.
7. Scan each row below the header.
8. Parse debit and credit.
9. Compute the absolute net amount.
10. Apply the candidate business rules.
11. Store qualifying rows in arrays.
12. Trim the arrays to the actual candidate count.
13. Sort the candidates descending by amount.
14. Run recursive subset search.
15. Delete any existing Open Items sheet.
16. Create a fresh output sheet.
17. Write the result headers.
18. Write selected rows or all candidates.
19. Add total and status information.
20. Autofit the output columns.
21. Show the completion message.
92. Why the output is useful for audit and review
The output preserves enough detail to trace each selected row back to the source worksheet. That is important because a reconciliation tool is only useful if users can verify why a row was selected. The voucher number and source row are enough to support that traceability in a normal Excel workflow.

This makes the output practical for follow-up work.
93. Why the macro is repeatable
Given the same data and the same target, the same result should be produced every time. There is no randomness in sorting or matching. That repeatability matters because financial workflows often need consistency across repeated runs and different reviewers.

It also makes testing much easier.
94. Why the candidate filter is also a computational filter
Rows removed by IsOpenCandidate are removed from the recursive search too. That means the accounting filter has a direct effect on runtime. In other words, business logic and algorithmic performance are tightly linked in this macro.

The more accurate the candidate filtering, the more manageable the search becomes.
95. Why heuristics are acceptable in spreadsheet reconciliation
Spreadsheet data often lacks structured status flags and clean metadata. A practical tool must therefore rely on clues such as the description text and the debit/credit pattern. The macro does that. It does not claim perfect semantic understanding; it simply uses the clues available to build a useful candidate pool.

That is a realistic approach for workbook automation.
96. Why the macro is row-local rather than relational
Each candidate is treated as a row-level item with its own amount and metadata. The macro does not need to reason about foreign keys or multi-table relationships. That is appropriate because the reconciliation problem is row-based and the workbook structure is likely row-based too.

Row-local logic keeps the solver simple and transparent.
97. Why the macro behaves well in a controlled template
Its assumptions are clear: fixed headers, known row structure, positive candidate amounts, and a consistent open-item vocabulary. In a controlled workbook, those assumptions are strengths because they keep the macro predictable and reliable. The macro does not need to be universal to be valuable.

Template specificity often improves operational quality.
98. Why the macro can be considered production-minded
It validates input structure, handles messy numeric formats, excludes likely non-open items, cleans output state, and restores Excel alerts after deleting the prior result sheet. Those are signs that the author thought about real use, not just algorithm correctness.

That makes the code feel like a production support tool rather than a toy example.
99. Final technical assessment
This VBA macro is a disciplined open-items reconciliation solver. It finds candidate accounting rows, normalizes and sorts them, searches for an exact subset that matches a target total, and writes a clean, traceable report sheet. Its strengths are robustness, traceability, and practical accounting logic. Its main weakness is the expected exponential cost of exact subset search when the candidate pool becomes too large.

Within a controlled workbook, it is a solid and useful solution.
100. Concise conceptual summary
The macro is saying: “From this worksheet, find the rows that look like unresolved items, then see whether some combination of them adds exactly to the target amount I need.” That is the right mental model for the code. It is a workbook-native solver built for reconciliation rather than a general-purpose data tool.

Everything else in the macro supports that goal.
101. Why the design is believable in real accounting work
The code does not pretend the data is clean. It assumes messy descriptions, formatted numbers, and correction entries. It does not pretend matching is simple. It uses recursive search and pruning. It does not hide the result. It writes a clear output sheet whether or not the exact match exists.

That realism is what makes the macro operationally credible.
102. Why the output sheet is the right end point
The user does not just need the answer in memory. The user needs a visible, reviewable worksheet that can be checked, saved, shared, or adjusted. The Open Items sheet serves that purpose. It converts the solver result into a human-readable reconciliation report.

That is the correct endpoint for this type of macro.
103. Why this is a workflow tool rather than a formula
It does not live in one cell. It runs through the workbook, applies business rules, solves a combinatorial problem, and writes a new sheet. That makes it a workflow tool. The output is the visible artifact of the process, not just a value displayed in a cell.

This is exactly the kind of thing VBA is used for in practice.
104. Why the macro is easy to explain to a user
The explanation is straightforward: select the sheet, run the macro, enter the target amount, and check the Open Items sheet. The complexity of the matching happens inside the code, not in the user interaction. That is a good design for a finance tool because it keeps the user path simple.

The user does not need to understand the recursion to use the macro.
105. Why this macro has clear boundaries
It is designed for a specific worksheet structure and a specific type of accounting problem. It is not meant to solve every reconciliation scenario. That boundary is important because it keeps the logic focused and prevents the macro from becoming vague or overgeneralized.

Clear scope is a strength here, not a limitation.
106. Why the macro is more efficient than manual trial-and-error
Manually trying to find combinations of vouchers that equal a target total can be slow and error-prone. This macro automates the search, applies a consistent filter, and writes the result in a structured report. That saves time and reduces the chance of manual misselection.

In that sense, it provides real operational value.
107. Why the solution is useful even if not perfect
It may miss some items if the description filter is too strict, and it may become slower if the candidate set is large. But within the expected workbook context, it still does something highly useful: it turns a manual reconciliation task into a structured and repeatable process.

That is a strong practical outcome even without mathematical perfection.
108. Final takeaway
This macro is a focused, practical VBA reconciliation tool. It combines text filtering, monetary normalization, candidate sorting, recursive subset matching, and clean worksheet reporting into one workflow. Its strongest traits are its traceability and its direct support for exact open-item matching. Its main limitation is the inherent complexity of subset search, which is why the filtering stage matters so much.

Within its intended Excel template, it is a solid and well-structured solution.
